- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "I will be interested to see Bickham's roman letters. However, I'm not sure
    how decisive they will be on the issue of the influence of writing on type. This
    is because you yourself say that they are \"aspiring to typographic regularity
    and form.\" So it is hard to say that writing is in the driver's seat, as I read
    Noordzij as saying. \r\n\r\nAt any rate it is not worth arguing over what exactly
    Noordzij meant to say, as he will no doubt explain himself further in other writings.
    \r\n\r\nWhat is important that I think you are missing is that type designers
    such as Jenson and Griffo were not only emulating the best scribal hands of their
    day, but they were also changing them decisively. And the changes, though subtle,
    decisively improved readability. That is why I think the issue is testable. Because
    I think some types are inherently more readable because of human psychology, and
    their increased readability has to do with their departure from writing.\r\n\r\nMy
    view on this, I now remember, is influenced not by Morrison, but by the analysis
    of Harry Carter, who I think you will agree had a very good knowledge of the scribal
    culture as well as type design. And he, it seems, didn't have Morrison's ego or
    commercial agenda.   \r\n\r\nCarter talks about how Felice Feliciano started drawing
    letters with reference to mathematical schemes. These were artificial, Carter
    says, but they performed a key service of separating the idea of an ideal form
    for letters from the actual movement of the pen. The impact of this separation
    on the punch cutter \r\n\r\n\"was good: he was at liberty to use files and gravers
    to express the ideal forms and no longer obliged to copy the way in which penmen
    had tried to approximate to them. \r\n\r\n\"I have said that the best masters
    of letter-cutting had the greatest respect for penstrokes and judged type by their
    fidelity to them. ...I am now arguing that ...using his particular tools to produce
    mentally conceived images makes the best type; and I think this is right also.
    ...It is because [Griffo] understood what I am trying to say so well ...that the
    Roman font ...[for]De Aetna was decisive in shaping the printer's alphabet. \r\n\r\nThe
    small letters are very well made to conform with the genuinely antique capitals
    by emphasis on long straight strokes and fine serifs and to harmonize in curvature
    with them. The strokes are thinner than those of Jenson and his school... . The
    balancing of the weight of capitals and lower case is helped by making the capitals
    line at the head with the undersides of the serifs of lower-case ascenders, not
    with their tops as Jenson's do. The letters look narrower than Jenson's but in
    fact are a little wider because the short ones are bigger [x-height ?], and the
    effect of narrowness makes the face suitable for octavo pages.\"\r\n\r\nCarter
    earlier praised Jenson as producing types that have been unsurpassed for \"folios
    and quartos\", but pointedly reserves the honor for octavos to Griffo as producing
    the model for later types.\r\n\r\nThis point about smaller sizes is actually quite
    important. Scribes, at least in the stuff I have seen, produced letters of larger
    size than the 8-12 that in some tests--not ideal tests, but extensive ones--are
    most readable. Not larger, not smaller. Part of the readability comes just from
    being able to size things for the eye, rather than the hand. But all of those
    changes that Griffo introduced, and I believe others that Jenson started, affect
    not only the regularity of type, but also its evenness of color. These are also
    important to readability, in my view. \r\n\r\nIn your manuscript example--which
    is trying but failing to emulate the readability of type--the r's for example
    have significantly different widths, and the spacing is not the even look of type.
    Also it is more upright than other humanist writing I have seen, again going against
    the tendency of the hand, and following type. But also I think that--contrary
    to Carter--it is probably impossible by the broad pen of fixed width to get the
    size and ratios of vertical stems, horizontal stems, diagonal stems, widths of
    arches, thickness and shapes of serifs that Jenson and Griffo have. The even rhythm
    and color of extended text in type is superior to almost all written work, to
    my eyes.  \r\n\r\nI certainly have not made a study of these early types to be
    able to a good case for my view here. But I do note that Harry Carter does decisively
    disagree with your view, John, that \"Griffo\u2019s innovations are pretty minor
    and well within the influence of the formal humanist book hand.\" The differences
    are subtle, but Carter clearly thinks that the differences, some of which he notes,
    are major. \r\n\r\nI think they are not only major, but that Jenson and Griffo
    made type significantly more readable than writing.  "

    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\"he was at liberty to use files and gravers to express the ideal forms\"
    [\u2026] \"using his particular tools to produce mentally conceived images makes
    the best type\"\r\n\r\nCarter might think this is right and good, but I doubt
    if it shows a good understanding of how type design and innovation in fact work
    or what kinds of things motivate and guide the kind of extended featural manipulation
    files and gravers make possible. \r\n\r\n\r\nAnd about the \"Jenson and Griffo
    made type significantly more readable than writing\" thing, why is it not enough
    to say, for instance: Jenson and Griffo\u2019s typographical manipulations consolidated
    the robustness of the affordances proper to readable writing at text sizes and
    extended it to smaller sizes?"
  created: '2007-09-24 22:51:06'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">why is it not enough to say, for instance: Jenson and Griffo\u2019s typographical
    manipulations consolidated the robustness of the affordances proper to readable
    writing at text sizes and extended it to smaller sizes?\r\n\r\nBecause it goes
    beyond small size. If you draw a stem with a fixed width nib, such as I understand
    they were using, fixed say at 30 degrees, you are going to get vertical stems
    and the thick part of vertical arches the same width. And the diagonals going
    left to right, top to bottom are going to have a thicker cross section than the
    verticals. The horizontal serifs are going to be asymmetrical, fatter to the right.
    \r\n\r\nIn Griffo's Bembo type, if it is similar to most later text types, which
    I am supposing it is as their ancestor, then none of this holds. The vertical
    arches are thicker than the vertical stems. The thick diagonals are about the
    same cross section as the vertical stems, not significantly fatter. And the serifs
    are fairly symmetrical, tapering similarly on both sides. All of this, with the
    possible exception of the serifs, helps to have even color in the letters. \r\n\r\nMy
    conjectures about readability of the printers types of Jenson and Griffo are just
    that, conjectures. But the reality is that the punch cutter was creating forms
    that the scribe would not have done in the normal course of events, and might
    not be able to do at all, even rotating the pen continually.  \r\n\r\nThe underlying
    point which I think Noordzij doesn't note sufficiently, is that what is good for
    the eye to read and what is easy for the hand to write are two different things.
    Sometimes they match, and some times--like slant--they diverge. Printer's type
    liberated designers to be able to meet the needs of the readers eye, rather than
    the convenience of the writer's hand. "

    to readable writing at text sizes.\""

- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>I will be interested to see Bickham\u2019s roman letters. However,
    I\u2019m not sure how decisive they will be on the issue of the influence of writing
    on type. This is because you yourself say that they are \u201Caspiring to typographic
    regularity and form.\u201D So it is hard to say that writing is in the driver\u2019s
    seat, as I read Noordzij as saying.</em>\r\n\r\nStop arguing with Noordzij and
    argue with me damnit! :)\r\n\r\nThe fact is that the characteristic forms we find
    in Baskerville's types, by which we recognise them and assign them to particular
    categorisation distinct from preceding types, were worked out with <em>pens</em>
    by <em>writing</em> masters as part of an overall aesthetic within contemporary
    text culture. Yes, they are very carefully written forms that emulate the generic
    regularity of type, <em>but they do not resemble any existing typefaces</em>.
    Only later are these styles of written letters translated into type by Baskerville.
    So there is a kind of symbiosis: written letters aspiring to the regularity of
    typographic letters, but introducing new forms, new ductus and new details, inspiring
    new typefaces. This shouldn't surprise us <em>if</em> we keep before us the idea
    of a single text culture in which writing and typography are parallel means of
    creating text, evolving at different paces but tending to harmony.\r\n\r\nRe.
    the earlier development and relationship of the typographic forms of Jenson and
    Griffo to the contemporary formal bookhand, I've never suggested that typographic
    letters were or should be direct representations of writing. There is always a
    question of scale which must never be ignored, and as soon as you start reducing
    the size of letters you need to make changes to their form, weight and spacing.
    I think we agree on this (Peter too), and perhaps we can say that this is what
    constitutes the distinctive character of typographic letters (at least in the
    Latin script tradition; some of the world's writing systems, including Hebrew
    and Arabic, have traditions of micrography in which the proportions that we associate
    with typographic letters are worked out with very small pens).\r\n\r\nYou say:
    <em>The differences are subtle, but Carter clearly thinks that the differences,
    some of which he notes, are major.</em>\r\n\r\nMy point is that they are, indeed,
    subtle, and can only be considered 'major' in the limited context of a history
    that only looks at types and not at a broader text culture. There's nothing wrong
    with such a history, if it acknowledges it's limitations, and I'm quite happy
    to argue the importance of quite small details in different typefaces. God is
    in the details. But the 'big picture' has to include everything that contributes
    to contemporary text culture, not just one technology. I'm sensitive to this issue
    at the moment, because based on the fantasy of typography as an entirely separate,
    progressive field, some of the world's writing systems are being reduced to type
    in ways that are completely cut off from the mainstream of text creation in their
    respective cultures. So to me this isn't an abstract historical discussion about
    renaissance Italy and Georgian England, but about looking at text cultures as
    holistic phenomena."

- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, when I said \u201CWhat matters is that in each domain \u2014 scribal
    writing and typography \u2014 affordances be robust,\u201D I was refering back
    to my statement about a logic of visual wordform resolution and a logic of discrimination
    affordances and a logic of perceptual salience and cue value. \r\n\r\nReading
    relies, so it seems to me, on the products of writing or typographical culture
    supporting \u2014 that is, \"affording\" \u2014 rapid, automatic visual wordform
    resolution, by facilitating \u2014 that is, \u201Caffording\u201D \u2014 accurate
    and unencumbered discrimination of criterial letter parts. Controlling salience
    and optimizing cue-value through manipulation of proportion, weight, contrast
    and construction are type\u2019s ways to go about this. Even colour strikes me
    as not much more than a by-product or diagnostic tool in this regard.\r\n\r\nThinking
    about perception in terms of affordances was pioneered by the American perceptual
    scientists James and Eleanor Gibson in the 1960s and 1970s and has proved immensely
    fruitful since.\r\n\r\nTo say that what matters is that, in each domain, affordances
    be robust, or to speak of extension and consolidation in this context, strikes
    me as more descriptive,  historical, generous and true. Try substituting your
    braod, experiential \u201Creadability\u201D for my more technical and scientifically
    tractable \u201Caffordances\u201D and you might see what I am after."
  created: '2007-09-25 04:57:07'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "I should have asked about the meaning of 'affordance' here in the first place
    before responding, as it is a technical term. Can you give some examples of this
    concept other than in reading? \r\n\r\nI do think that even color is more than
    a <em>by-product</em> of \"accurate and unencumbered discrimination of criterial
    letter parts.\" To me it actually a force promoting unencumbered discrimination.
    When color is uneven <strong>s</strong>u<strong>c</strong>h a<strong>s</strong>
    <strong>t</strong>hi<strong>s</strong>, th<strong>e</strong> u<strong>n</strong><strong>e</strong>ve<strong>n</strong><strong>n</strong>es<strong>s</strong>
    d<strong>i</strong><strong>s</strong><strong>t</strong>ra<strong>c</strong><strong>t</strong>s
    from identification of the salient characteristics of the letters and words.\r\n\r\nTo
    put it another way, uneven color is a kind of 'noise' that interferes with the
    'signals' from salient letter characteristics. Getting the color even reduces
    the noise and affords ready identification.     \r\n\r\n"

- author:

  body: Bringhurst raises the interesting question of how reading might be affected
    by the link between visual cognitive processes and manual motor memory in the
    brain. In other words, does it affect the way you read if you are also a writer?
    It is an interesting question, stemming from his observation that for much of
    our literate history everyone who read also wrote.

- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "Peter:<em> There is, in that sense, no science [of] readability or legibility,
    just experiential reports. Beyond that there is...</em>\r\n\r\n...still no science,
    just performance statisitics for specific fonts.\r\n\r\n<em>Controlling salience
    and optimizing cue-value through manipulation of proportion, weight, contrast
    and construction are type\u2019s ways to go about this [affordance].</em>\r\n\r\nHow
    can affordance be \"technically and scientifically tractable\" when it is tied
    to such typeface-specific, multi-variant concepts? Suppose, for instance, that
    one wished to determine an optimum \"proportion\" -- i.e. generate a performance
    curve -- for x-height. One might produce half-a-dozen variants of a typeface,
    and discover that different versions worked better in different settings, depending
    on size, leading and line length. Then, applying the same procedure to a different
    typeface, one would discover that variant \"A\" of typeface \"y\" worked better
    in type specs 1 and 2, whereas variant C of typeface \"z\" was more suited to
    those specs. And so on. \r\n\r\nFor \"construction\", would it be possible to
    discover the optimum-affordance combination of single and double-story \"a\" and
    \"g\"? The Futura scheme would probably not fare well, but how would News Gothic
    do against Helvetica? Tests would have to be made of both Helvetica vs. Helvetica
    with a News Gothic style g, and News Gothic vs. News Gothic with a Helveticized
    g, and then test the winners against each other. \r\n\r\nBut is the quest for
    a theoretical gestalt logic of reading similar to theoretical physics, in a technical
    and scientific sense?\r\nThere is no particle accelerator, no mathematical equations.
    \r\nPeter, what you are proposing is aesthetic or design theory. Nothing wrong
    with that. Although it is based on biological research it is not scientific, it's
    more like a theory of marketing based on market testing.\r\n\r\nYou say, \"What
    matters is that in each domain \u2014 scribal writing and typography \u2014 affordances
    be robust.\" Why does that matter? What is the goal, to understand how reading
    works, or to make scientific rules for design?\r\n\r\n \r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"

- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill,  From <em>Has Psychology a Future</em> by Eleanor J[ack] Gibson: \u201CAffordance,
    a term coined by J J Gibson (1979), refers to a reciprocal relationship between
    an animal and its environment The environment provides opportunities, and the
    animal provides (or does not provide) an action system that can utilize what the
    environment offers. Perceiving the relationship is perceiving an affordance, an
    animal-environment fit.\u201D \r\n\r\nEleanor Gibson uses the example of a child
    reaching for something, which involves, among other things, a expectation that
    the object it is reaching for affords being grasped.\r\n\r\nFrom:  <em>Gibson's
    Affordances</em> by James G. Greeno (Psychological Review 1994, Vol. 101, No.
    2, 336-342)\r\n\u201CThe idea is quite straightforward. In any interaction involving
    an agent with some other system, conditions that enable that interaction include
    some properties of the agent along with some properties of the other system. [\u2026]
    An affordance relates attributes of something in the environment to an interactive
    activity by an agent who has some ability, and an ability relates attributes of
    an agent to an interactive activity with something in the environment that has
    some affordance.\u201D\r\n\r\nHere the example is \u201Cthe affordance of an aperture
    for a person to walk from one side of a partition to the other. The affordance
    provided by an aperture is a function of its width, and the ability of a person
    to move through the aperture depends on the person's width.\r\n\r\nMy point has
    been that, involved in reading are at the very least <em>perceptual discrimination</em>
    affordances and <em>visual wordform resolutional</em> affordances which differ
    from simple <em>letter-identity resolutional</em> affordances.\r\n\r\n\r\nNick,
    perceptual discrimination affordances and visual wordform resolutional affordances
    are indeed tied to a type-specific, multi-variant system of interactive parameters.
    So the most that can be expected might indeed be performance testing. However,
    it might be useful to think of performance testing as exploring the \u201Caffordance
    structure\u201D of the font or font system when it comes to reading. Each font,
    I'd think, has its own optimums, ranges of affordances and thresholds. ( I use
    the plural, because fonts behave differently in different contexts of use.) And
    affordances change.\r\n\r\nBut I still think that if we dig deeper into these
    affordance structures we will find a deeper logic of salience (for instance),
    which is less plastic than you seem to want to think, but more forgiving than
    you think I want to find. It is a logic more characterized by ranges and thresholds
    than by sharp peaks flanked by dramatic drops.\r\n\r\n\r\nJohn, you asked:  \u201Cdoes
    it affect the way you read if you are also a writer?\u201D That's a whole \u2019nother
    ball of wax, but I think writing encourages an intimacy with the featural structure
    of the script, that has to impact what our neural receptive fields become alert
    to or \u2018trained on\u2019. Type-design might do the same, but perhaps only
    if it is informed by knowledge of ductal logic.\r\n\r\n\r\nDavid, from Jasper's
    view of history I might learn the importance of constructing my own take on history.
    I think we might all agree that the tools of the punch-cutter and the spline-bender
    afford a broader scope of featureal manipulation, that has it's uses. The problem
    is in how best to state what the products of these efforts at featureal adjustment
    represent within the broader worlds of writing culture and affordances."
  created: '2007-09-25 16:38:46'
- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "Peter: <em>But I still think that if we dig deeper into these affordance
    structures we will find a deeper logic of salience (for instance), which is less
    plastic than you seem to want to think, but more forgiving than you think I want
    to find.</em>\r\n\r\nWithout supporting facts, that's wishful thinking. \r\nIt
    would require cognitive scientists to do a humungous amount of research testing
    multiple typefaces and typeface variables against demographic variables. Frankly,
    I don't think that will happen. Pardon my cynicism, and I wish I could be proven
    wrong. Funding goes to in-house development projects, such as Microsoft's Cleartype
    program, which is a productization and branding of a MS-centric notion of readability
    -- or to dyslexic projects where novice type designers proceed from subjective
    assumptions to design typefaces for poor readers from scratch, without testing
    to see how existing faces perform. \r\n\r\n"

- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "Further to my investigation into the era-specific parameters of word-spacing,
    I've reset some text from a 1951 book I picked up over the weekend.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Time_6423.jpg]\r\nThe
    original is 11/18 Linotype Janson, on a 29 pica measure.\r\nQuite a bit of gain.\r\n.\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Time2_5677.jpg]\r\nDuplicating
    the specs (top) with a digital Janson (Kis) in InDesign with Paragraph Composer,
    default H&J settings, produces extremes of wordspacing, between different parts
    of the text. In order to match the original, it is necessary to increase the Desired
    Word Spacing to 150% (bottom).\r\n.\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Time3_6285.jpg]\r\nResetting
    in Minion Pro with default H&J settings, the effect is quite different. Tighter
    and smoother within lines of text, but with more disjunction between lines: \"railings\"--a
    90 degree rotation of the \"picket fence\" phenomenon.\r\n\r\nThere seems to be
    an intimate relationship between type design, typesetting technology, and typographic
    norms (of setting and reading), which goes some way to explaining why fifty years
    ago people were comfortable with a wordspace that was 150% of what it is now.
    As historiography, there is perhaps a parallel between the duration of typographic
    norms/\"laws\" and the duration of scientific laws."

  created: '2007-09-26 00:04:09'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask1_6576.jpg]\r\nThe images in this
    post are all taken from George Bickham's <em> The Universal Penman</em>, compiled
    and published in various parts and editions between 1733 and 1741, i.e. more than
    a decade <em>before</em> John Handy cut type punches in these styles at the direction
    of John Baskerville. Bickham published not only engraved examples of his own writing,
    but also that of more than a dozen contemporary writing masters. The images shown
    here are by various hands, including Emanuel Austin, John Bickham, Joseph Champion,
    William Leekey, Samual Vaux, and George Bickham himself. Other plates in <em>
    The Universal Penman</em> show the same styles written by other writers, demonstrating
    that these styles of roman and italic letters were a mature and standard part
    of the repertoire of the 18th century English writing masters in the first half
    of the century. \r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask2_6352.jpg]\r\nThis
    image shows the basic character of the style, as written by John Bickham in 1736.
    Note the open lower bowl of the italic g, a feature by which Baskerville's types
    are most easily recognised.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask3_5664.jpg]\r\nThis
    is not type. This is carefully written text that wants to be type. But it can't
    be type, because the typeface doesn't exist yet. This is text waiting for Baskerville's
    types, and in the meantime written by hand.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask6_4755.jpg]\r\nHere
    is the context for the previous image. This shows well the nature of the harmonious
    text aesthetic that this kind of roman letter is created to fit within, and nicely
    relates it to contemporary illustration in the versal cap. Even in the midst of
    neo-classicism, the English didn't completely lose their taste for gothick letters,
    which remained part of the basic repertoire of any writing master, but used flourishes
    to incorporate this style of lettering withing the larger asethetic.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/basktomb1_4434.jpg]\r\nThis
    practice is demonstrated to full effect in this illustration. [There will be more
    about tombs in a later post, once I have compiled some images of even earlier
    lettering in the English roman and italic styles from gravestones and memorial
    tablets.]\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask4_3575.jpg]\r\nAnother
    example of the roman and italic styles, written by Emanuel Austin in 1736.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/bask5_4931.jpg]\r\nAnd
    another, written by Joseph Champion in 1738.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xB6What all these images
    show is a model of formal roman and italic letters <em>worked out with a pen</em>
    and fully mature more than a decade before Baskerville produced the first typefaces
    in these styles. This does not undermine Baskerville's achievement -- the types
    show innovations appropriate to their medium and scale, and the careful selection
    of features to be locked into single forms from among the variety possible within
    the style is no mean feat --, but it very seriously undermines the idea that Baskerville's
    types are in any way derived from Caslon's. Baskerville may well have considered
    the latter in technical terms, examining their fit, alignment, etc. But the style
    of his types is exactly that developed by the writing masters. It is only possible
    to draw any kind of direct line between the design of Caslon's types and those
    of Baskerville by willfully ignoring all this writing."

    does have a vertical stress, so that is indeed a stylistic influence that I was
    not aware of. Was the same thing going on in signage of the day? In stone carving?
    \r\n\r\nTo me these don't have quite the same feel of Baskerville, in particular
    the symmetrical neo-classical arches.  \r\n\r\n> It is only possible to draw any
    kind of direct line between the design of Caslon\u2019s types and those of Baskerville
    by willfully ignoring all this writing.\r\n\r\nCaslon is *one* direct line of
    influence on Baskerville, as he himself says, and supported by the evidence I
    gave. But there are other lines of influence as well leading into Baskerville's
    work. As I said from the beginning, I assumed that increased contrast was an influence
    of the newer pens and styles. Vertical stress is there, but I'm wondering now
    if engraving was an influence here on both the pen and type. \r\n\r\nI know this
    is not your position, but Noordzij's claim seemed to be that writing in particular--the
    moving front--drives everything. And that--which was part of the essay that Peter
    started this thread with--still seems to me in a lot of doubt, and not settled
    by these examples. As you point out, there was a whole culture of letters: painted
    signs, stone carving, engraving and type as well as writing. Probably looking
    at all of it would be necessary to sort out the influences in more detail, if
    it is even possible.  "
  created: '2007-09-26 06:07:22'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bickham didn't call his book <em>The Universal Engraver</em>, he called it
    <em>The Universal Penman</em> and it was intended as an exemplar of written forms
    to inspire and instruct writers.\r\n\r\nI think the basic point is well made,
    in any case: the design aesthetic that informs Baskerville's type was well established
    over a decade before those types were made. It was part of a broader text culture
    with a distinctive canon of styles and forms based on the work of writing masters.
    A very similar thing, it should be noted, was happening in France, resulting in
    Fournier's types. Notable in this canon is the absence of even the latter styles
    of oldstyle types derived from the renaissance and mannerist line, of which Caslon's
    types were the last flowering. The text aesthetic developed in England in the
    18th century comes out of vernacular written, inscribed, engraved and cast letterforms,
    and is only latterly translated into type by Baskerville, Fry, Martin, and Austin.\r\n\r\nThe
    pen has in its favour the ready speed with which a practiced writer can produce
    shapes. It also has in its favour the sheer volume of handwritten text compared
    to engraving. So I find it much easier to believe that writing led the way, in
    18th century England at any rate, than engraving, which was very seldom a medium
    of expression and almost always a medium of reproduction."

- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "John, the samples raise as many questions as they solve. I think Bill's question
    about sources is apt. The relation of your images to writing with the flexible-nibbed
    pointed pen can\u2019t be on the order of facsimilies or perfect imitations of
    actual writng. The oblique strokes in the w and v of the romans give this away,
    because in writing with the pointed pen expansion has to be perpendicular to the
    angle of the pen, and not perpendicular to the direction of the stroke, unless
    the pen is rotated. \r\n\r\nThe engravings have to be examples of adapting the
    conventional contrast of received type to the conditions of writing with the flexible
    nibbed pen.\r\n\r\nWhat you seem to miss Bill, is that the stroke of the pointed,
    flexible nibbed pen is more than a stylistic influence, or an influence among
    influences, but provides a contrast manipulation paradigm that is fundamentally
    different in kind to that which came befor, in other words it\u2019s <em>genotypical</em>.
    Its sets new parameters for contrast maniplation, which become also foundational
    to the clarendons and the egyptiennes, and all kind of other lettering efforts.\r\n\r\nAnd
    Nick, in your sample spacing <em>is</em> the parameter, but I agree that there
    are era-specific ways of handling it. Also, I recognize that there seems to be
    an intimate relationship between type design, typesetting technology, typographic
    norms and perceptual processing affordances. However I don\u2019t know what \u2018typographic
    norms of reading\u2019 are.\r\n\r\nRelative to your sample I'd predict visual
    wordform resolution is still robust but sense-following tends toward a kind of
    rigorous mechanical sequential engagement with each and every word. \r\n\r\nThe
    optical effects of tightening spacing that your manipulations evoke are what practitioners
    who know how saliencies work, might expect."

    of the gothick style in the repertoire of mid-18th century writing masters is
    actually quite surprising given how little respected the mediaeval styles in art
    and architecture were during this period. "

  created: '2007-09-26 19:03:16'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>However, the similarities in structure of Baskerville and Caslon
    leave me convinced that Caslon was an important influence.</em>\r\n\r\nAn influence
    on what? I don't think you can claim that they were an influence on the new style
    of type, because the styles are so different and, in any case, Baskerville's types
    are in essentials identical to the George Shelley's alphabet of 1715 as shown
    in the plate that James provides. Some letters are different -- the Q, thanfully
    -- but they are also different from Caslon's and I suspect if one dug through
    enough exemplars from the writing masters one would find a model for every form
    in Baskerville's types. Why? Because the forms can all be worked out with a split
    nib pen -- even if a typical typographic contrast pattern sometimes requires rotation
    of the pen, which was not unheard of as Bodoni's swash letters make plain -- ,
    and these guys had been doing this for decades. I doubt if there was any workable
    shape that had not been fully explored with a pen by the time Baskerville came
    to cast it in type.\r\n\r\nThe trouble with trying to make a structural link between
    Baskerville's types and Caslon's evidence of influence or emulation is that you
    should first remove from the equation the structural similarities that are common
    to >90% of Latin text faces, that are accounted for by adjustments proper to the
    scale of the letters. Baskerville may have looked at Caslon's types to gain an
    understanding of what makes type work, because they were the most readily available
    samples, but he may well have relied on John Handy's expertise to provide that
    understanding. It is important to bear this in mind, because when we say that
    Baskerville 'designed' these types we run the risk of imagining an anachronistic
    industrial design process. Handy would have cut punches according to the knowledge
    of his craft, based on some form of model provided by Baskerville. This model
    would not have been 'drawings', of the kind that we are familiar with from much
    later, mechanical punch cutting processes. What were they? I think they were almost
    certainly pen-written letters of the kind shown in Shelley and Bickham's writing
    manuals, which Baskerville, as a writing master, would have been able to produce
    with ease and speed. Handy would have interpreted the letters to make them work
    at the sizes of type specified by Baskerville (just as, two centuries later, August
    Rosenberger interpreted in metal the written and drawn letters provided by Hermann
    Zapf).\r\n\r\nBaskerville did emulate Caslon, but primarily in the still novel
    notion of making original types in England, rather than importing them from the
    continent, as had been the norm for almost three hundred years. But Caslon based
    his types on the style of those imported types, while Baskerville based his on
    indigenous lettering styles.\r\n\r\nWhen Baskerville speaks of Caslon 'leaving
    room for improvement', I don't think he is referring merely to certain technical
    deficiencies such as poor vertical alignment and inconsistent serif weight, but
    also to the inappropriateness of Caslon's types to the neo-classical text aesthetic.
    The room for improvement was the gulf between the text culture of Holland a hundred
    years previous and the text culture of contemporary Britain."
  created: '2007-09-26 19:13:28'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>If you can assume that Baskerville was lying about being influenced
    by Caslon...</em>\r\n\r\nI don't think he was lying: I don't think he said it
    at all. I've read and re-read the statement, especially now that James has provided
    it in its original state, and I don't see anything in it that suggests that Baskerville
    is saying that the form of his types is influenced by Caslon's. He says that Caslon
    left a 'fairer copy for his emulation than any other master' (which seems intentionally
    ironic given that Baskerville was the (writing) master and his types are so clearly
    modeled the styles developed by his fellow writing masters), but does not say
    that he in fact emulated this copy. All he says he has done is to produce roman
    and italic types, in contrast to the great variety of types that Caslon was producing.
    There is something in it of the brash but nervous new competitor paying tribute
    to an established, monopolistic firm: 'Yes, of course Microsoft Windows is great
    and the world owes Bill Gates a great obligation, and we've personally learned
    a lot from Microsoft. But we do feel that they perhaps try to do too much in their
    operating system, and there is plenty of room in the market for a smaller, faster
    system. So here is what we've made. It doesn't do everything that Windows does,
    but what it does do we think it does better. We're not setting out to compete
    directly with Microsoft; indeed, we only seek a small share of the reputation
    (and market), from an area of software development that proves accidentally to
    have been the object of our mutual pursuit (please don't crush us).'"

  created: '2007-09-26 20:16:06'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "David: <em>So, doesn\u2019t this imply that greek could not so easily go
    through the transitional needle as Latin could?</em>\r\n\r\nI mentioned this discussion
    to Robert Bringhurst, who suggests that Baskerville's Greek is not so novel as
    is generally regarded, but that it has earlier precedence in the 16th century
    types of Stefano Niccolini da Sabbio, with which I am unfamiliar, and Simon de
    Coline's augustin Greeks, which I must have seen but can't recall.\r\n\r\nBickham
    includes a Greek plate at the end of <em>The Universal Penman</em>, in which the
    conventional shapes, including ligatures, of the old Byzantine cursive style are
    written with a split nib pen. Minus most of the ligatures, and with greater regularity
    of proportion, this approach is the basis for the Didot, Bodoni and Leipzig Greek
    types.\r\n\r\n[Bickham also has a highly entertaining Arabic, in which the horizontal
    strokes must have been written from left-to-right because you can't push a pointed
    split-nib pen. It's almost the invention of a new style of Arabic script, but
    it doesn't quite work, despite some very beautiful individual shapes.]"
  created: '2007-09-26 20:50:30'
- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "I would think that in England the innovations came from writing, because
    of the monopoly on book printing exercised by the Company of Stationers, and government
    restrictions on newspaper and pamphlet publishing by means of repressive taxation.
    Surely these restrictive practices would have created and maintained an intensely
    conservative type culture, which certainly seems to be the case prior to changes
    in the copyright law in 1774 -- opening up book publishing, and the reduction
    of newspaper tax in 1836.\r\n\r\nThis is not to discount the influence of innovators,
    but such people would be few and far between, outsiders of sufficient committment
    and wealth to work independently, John Baskerville, for instance. And what success
    did he have? Outside of notoriety, precious little, except perhaps in France.
    Other typographic outsiders were at work in the 18th century in Scotland, where,
    notably, the law, in 1727(?) reduced copyright to a few years, not perpetual as
    in England, and book culture consequently was not so turgid as in England.\r\n\r\nThe
    invention of lithography and its vogue in the early 19th century contributed to
    a loosening up of the lettering arts in general, as can be seen from the fashion
    for illustrations of lettering made out of rustic briars etc."
  created: '2007-09-26 20:59:37'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Wait a minute, maybe I shouldn't have plead guilty, I mean pleaded wrong
    in response to Peter's argument.  \r\n\r\nI just read that the pointed nib pen
    came into use in the 17th century. But both punch cutting and engraving came into
    use at the end of the 15 and into the 16th century. And in both of these you can
    get the extreme thicks and thins. So you already have a 'contrast manipulation
    paradigm' in these. So why do you need the pen to start thinking of letters with
    more extreme thicks and thin? Maybe the pointed pen came into use to imitate printing
    and engraving, not the other way round. Its widespread use is evidently later.
    Were there high contrast engraved letters before the pointed nib pen? Did any
    of them have vertical stress? "

    column letters and other Roman inscriptions were layed out using brush lettering
    long before the 17th century.  The broadnib pen you speak of was the slotted version.
    Reed pens were in use in ancient Greece. They were not as efficient t holding
    ink though so you got dry spots at the end of strokes.\r\n\r\nChrisL"

  body: ">contrast template\r\n\r\nThe problem is that the contrast template was already
    there, from the broad-nibbed pen and modifications by type designers. Having vertical
    stress and greater contrast didn't change the basic rules--rules like thick verticals
    and thin horizontals, alternating thick thin on the W, or the tapering of strokes
    at joins of curve to straight. \r\n\r\nI can see the influence of the pointed
    nib on loopy scripts like Bickham, but not on romans. \r\n\r\nAnd with sans, later,
    you have a *reduction* in contrast, even when the pointed steel pen is most flourishing.\r\n\r\nThe
    'contrast template' of the pointed Chinese brush is, in comparison, radically
    different. Put any two characters from Griffo and from Bodoni side by side. Then
    put both beside a brushed Chinese character.   "

    all of which they had the freedom to alter contrast, and did. For example Caslon
    did high contrast faces for large sizes, lower for small sizes. \r\n\r\nTo be
    sure, different designers handle the thick to thin curves differently, but isn't
    that a design decision rather than any 'template' dictated by the pen?  "
  created: '2007-09-27 05:47:30'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill, I really think you are arguing against yourself. As you say, punchcutting
    and engraving technically permit high contrast, fine hairlines, etc. And yet the
    normative model of typographic letterforms from the 15th to the mid-18th century
    remained based on the translation contrast pattern of the broad-nib pen. It was
    not until after the development of the split-nib pen and its associated writing
    styles, based on an expansion contrast pattern, that type founders began to make
    types that were modeled on that pattern. The fact that it would have been technically
    possible to cut such types at an earlier date is notable precisely because no
    one chose to do so: the technology precedes the writing, but the writing precedes
    the application of the technology to those forms.\r\n\r\nIt is also interesting
    to note that between the translation model of the renaissance broad nib and the
    flourishing of the expansion model of the split nib in the 18th century, one finds
    the rotation model of the mannerist and early baroque writing masters. And yet
    one sees almost no influence of this different technique and, hence, different
    contrast pattern, in the roman text types of this period, which stick pretty doggedly
    to the translation model. The obvious explanation for this is that the mannerist
    writing masters did not provide a model for such types: they did not apply the
    new writing techniques to those kinds of letters. You do find some evidence of
    rotation in italics, though, and in the civilit\xE9 types which were modeled on
    the popular writing style of the time. In the 18th century, by contrast, we have
    writing masters such as Shelley explicitly providing models for typographic roman
    letterforms produced with the preferred tool of the day according to the preferred
    techniques of the day.\r\n\r\nSo not only do we have the evidence of type founders
    creating types that follow trends and models set by writing masters, we also <em>don't</em>
    see them creating types based on other potential models when these are not provided
    by the writing masters, despite the technical ability to make any shape that the
    cutting, punching and casting processes would permit. Overall, typography prior
    to the 20th century comes across as a very conservative field, following behind
    the much more dynamic development of new formal writing styles. There are good
    economic reasons for this: making type was a costly and time consuming business,
    while a writing master needed only a pen, some ink and some paper to develop and
    practice a new style.\r\n\r\n\r\n<em>I can see the influence of the pointed nib
    on loopy scripts like Bickham, but not on romans.</em>\r\n\r\nThen you're blind.
    Sorry, but there's no polite way to put it. From Baskerville to Didot to Bodoni
    the expansion contrast pattern is the defining basis of the new styles (much more
    than axis, by the way, which actually varied a lot more than the popular revivals
    indicate). Not only is it possible to emulate all these styles of types using
    a split nib pen, it is impossible to emulate them with any other kind of pen (presuming
    one is writing the strokes and not drawing the letter).\r\n\r\nThe lower contrast,
    sans serif letters were based on a different but still contemporary style of <em>drawn</em>
    lettering (see James' <em>The Nymph and the Grot</em>. No one is claiming that
    the split nib was the only tool used during this period, or that the styles of
    writing associated with it were the only styles of lettering either popular or
    invented during the period. But it was the only tool used for the principle styles
    of formal writing, and evidence of the broad nib is only found in the gothick."
  created: '2007-09-27 06:23:09'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>To be sure, different designers handle the thick to thin curves
    differently, but isn\u2019t that a design decision rather than any \u2019template\u2019
    dictated by the pen?</em>\r\n\r\nWho are these 'designers'? What was their process
    of design?\r\n\r\nI'm not sure who the first person was to describe himself as
    a 'type designer' or when, but I suspect it was quite late, and reflects a process
    of design within an industrial type manufacturing setting. As I wrote earlier,
    I'm pretty sure that Baskerville's design process consisted of sitting down and
    writing the style of letters that he wanted, and John Handy translated the written
    forms into type.\r\n\r\nWe know that, earlier, renaissance printers hired writing
    masters to produce models for italic types and or modeled their types on the writing
    of specific scribes, whose names are known to us.\r\n\r\nAnd I wonder what Griffo's
    design process was for the Aldine roman types? Obviously there is an evaluation
    of Jenson's types involved, but how did he -- and many future generations of 'designers'
    -- actually visualise the letters to be cut in the end of the bits of metal? It
    is very unlikely that he worked directly in the metal without a model -- the fact
    that Rudolf Koch did this was notably unconventional --, so what did the model
    look like, and how did he create it? I think we can be reasonably sure that he
    did not produce draughtsman's englarged drawings of a kind that only became necessary
    with the introduction of mechanism punchcutting. I think it is overwhelmingly
    likely that he used a broad nib pen and carefully wrote the forms he intended
    to translate into type, because of course he was not only a punchcutter, he was
    also a writer, just like every other literate person of his time. He may not have
    been a great calligrapher or a professional writing master, but he knew how to
    handle a pen just as he knew, as a punchcutter, how to translate those written
    models into metal and what adjustments to make for scale and technical limitations.\r\n\r\nInterestingly,
    Arrighi's italic types seem to have required fewer ligatures than Griffo's, suggesting
    that the professional writing master had better insights into how to adapt letters
    to the technology, while Griffo tried to follow a particular written model that
    obliged him to use more ligatures."

    he started out doing gravestones, and would have drawn those outlines before he
    started chiselling.\r\nHe was a precocious letterer and no doubt a multiple threat,
    like many a talented letter designer.\r\nAs a writing master early in his career,
    I suspect he would have used a fine nib to draw large exemplary letters, filling
    in the thick strokes.\r\nLook at the line weight of the flourishes in the Shelley
    specimen -- would not Shelley have used the same pen to outline the large letters?\r\n\r\nWas
    Baskerville also an artist/illustrator? Did he design the images for his Japanning
    business? It's a very graphic medium.\r\nI recall reading that he also had flash

    are not provided by the writing masters</em>\r\n\r\nWould they have been familiar
    with the geometrically constructed forms of the Roman du Roi?"

- author:
    name: James Mosley
  body: "I wonder if I can offer my own view of some of these topics without disrupting
    the thread (as sometimes seems to happen).\r\n\r\nA long time ago I put together
    an argument and some images under the title \u2018English vernacular', and I returned
    to the theme this year in <em>Forum</em>, the journal of Letter Exchange, and
    in papers at Bologna and at ATypI in Brighton.\r\n\r\nThe title is ironic, since
    \u2018English' refers to the round hand script of 1700 and after that was called
    English in France and other countries (<em>l'\xE9criture anglaise</em>), but derives
    from French, Dutch and, above all, Italian models. My hero then, as now, is the
    calligrapher Giovan Francesco Cresci, who introduced flowing writing using the
    flexible pointed pen in about 1560, and changed the shape of Western writing and
    typography.\r\n\r\nThe common charge against the new calligraphy is that it was
    not only unnatural (since Nature had ordained the broad pen) but that it was a
    perverse attempt to copy the brilliant work of copper plate engravers. That is
    only to say that the new generation of Italian (then Dutch and French) copy books
    was indeed engraved on copper and that the style was increasingly used in the
    lettering of maps and prints. I think stylish and brilliant copperplate models
    are to some extent responsible for the increasing contrast between thick and thin
    that appears in the bigger types of the 17th century (these have been noted in
    this thread), and I would add to this the tendency of their serifs to become thinner,
    the appearance of \u2018fish hook' serifs in the C, G and S of Dutch types like
    Van Dijck's (and their adoption in England) and the appearance of the calligraphic
    double-curve to the tail of capital R that can be seen in several 17th-century
    English types.\r\n\r\nIf the 18th-century \u2018English' style became a force
    to be reckoned with in the history of writing and type generally it was because
    of the increasing strength of British industrial power at home and the creation
    of a new maritime empire, based on trade, and held together with a web of paper
    documents, bills of lading, letters of credit, and so on, that needed to be clearly,
    accurately and impressively written. Hence the rise of the English writing masters,
    and sooner or later, their power to influence print. It is symbolic that this
    was achieved by a writing master who became a successful self-made industrialist
    in the boom town of Birmingham, miles from the tightly-knit, conservative book
    trade of London. Caslon, a Midlander like Baskerville, chose to join this conservative
    world and did very well for himself by catering for it competently, whereas Baskerville
    was notoriously seen by many Londoners as a strange being from outer space.\r\n\r\nIncidentally,
    Nick, the notion of British \u2018perpetual copyright' was not so much changed
    by law in 1774 as be discovered to be non-existent \u2013 but you are right in
    pointing to the legal case of this year as a key event which decided the matter.
    It generated a boom in competing editions of the same text for which new types
    and new wove paper were used as promotional features. And this opening up of the
    market for books, which fed off the rise of a new rich middle class, generated
    a demand for the new, distinctive, \u2018modern cut', types of the 1780s and after.\r\n\r\nIf
    I can marshal these arguments effectively, I shall do my best to use them in a
    new text of \u2018English vernacular'. And I shall be delighted to go on reading
    debates like this one that will help me to get things right.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"
  created: '2007-09-27 10:24:51'

- author:
    name: dberlow
  body: "Thanks for that Peter, the ascent/descent of type. To the point of how much
    more objective we can be about the history of our craft than the \"average\" historian,
    I think it goes on and on until we test the flexibility of the exact kinds of
    tines they made to be sure of 90% of what we think we know.\r\n\r\nI also like
    James' introduction of the relationship between the text type they were setting
    and the display type they were drawing or engraving to accompany it. I am comfortable
    setting or recommending the setting of text (8-10.99 pt) in oldstyle even if a
    transitional or a modern is set above it in the display size. Starting at 11 or
    12 pt. a mix such as this becomes dicey. But I am very uncomfortable setting or
    recommending an oldstyle above a modern at any size. \r\n\r\nSo I'm assuming these
    effects on form in visual wordform resolutional include our desire to see things
    more handwritten the smaller they are, (probably to a point) in the same way we
    are more comfortable with wood upon stone rather than the opposite, which gives
    me the creeps, worse even than oldstyle display above modern text.\r\n\r\nCheers!"

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">disrupting the thread\r\n\r\nAre you kidding? I would much rather read your
    knowledgeable analysis than my guessing. \r\n\r\nI have made no pretense of knowing
    the history of writing here. What I did react against was what seemed to me an
    exaggerated claim of Noordzij, that later type designers disregarded what earlier
    type designers did, and only looked to writing. Having spent recently a lot of
    time designing letters, I just don't find that plausible. I may have misinterpreted
    Noordzij, but my impression from reading both <em>Letter Letter</em> and <em>The
    Stroke</em> (translated by Peter) is that he has brilliant new insights on the
    development of letters, but that he at times claims they are the whole story rather
    than part of the story. \r\n\r\n>you are arguing against yourself. \r\n\r\nWell,
    yes, after Peter's argument I said I thought I was wrong, but then had second--or
    third--thoughts about that as I looked a little more into the history. Now that
    James confirms my guess that there <em>was</em> an influence of engraving on the
    adoption of the pointed, flexible pen, I am thinking now that the story is one
    of <em>interaction</em> between the crafting of letters in different media. \r\n\r\n>Then
    you\u2019re blind. Sorry, but there\u2019s no polite way to put it.  \r\n\r\nYou
    are, um, jumping to conclusions. My guess, which may well be wrong, is that the
    letters such as the Shelly letters James showed us are constructed, not written.
    By that I mean that they look carefully built up of many strokes, not truly 'written',
    with a continuously moving front. I see that Nick also has the same feeling. And
    if I read him rightly, David also sees them this way. Can you tell us more about
    how these letters were made, James?\r\n\r\nReading the Wikipedia article about
    the pointed nib, I see that you can't do a thick upstroke with the pointed nib,
    but have great control on the down strokes. I think I can see affects of that
    in the cursive styles, which are more written, but the Roman look to me constructed,
    and keeping largely to the earlier rules of where contrast should fall--but just
    made more vertical.  \r\n\r\nPeter wrote that there was an 'expansion contrast
    template'. Thinking about it further, I do see that the vertical stress would
    be more natural with the pointed nib, as you could bear down on the vertical down
    strokes, and in that sense he is right. However, you also have the tradition of
    'constructed' letters, from the 15th century on. I see an example of a vertical
    stress letter from the Romain du Roi, and I am wondering how early vertical stress
    'constructed' letters exist--do they exist before the pointed pen? \r\n\r\nSo
    I would concede to Peter and you at this point that the vertical stress seems
    more natural with the pointed pen. But I am wondering whether the point pen is
    following design ideas from earlier, with constructed letters, and letters created
    in other media: punch-cut, painted, stone carved, engraved. \r\n\r\n>I\u2019m
    not sure who the first person was to describe himself as a \u2019type designer\u2019
    or when, but I suspect it was quite late\r\n\r\nI know that applying the term
    'type designer' to Jenson is anachronistic, but I don't think it is misleading.
    As Harry Carter pointed out in the passage I quoted above, the earlier creators
    of printers type such as Jenson and Griffo were not simply copying writing, but
    were cutting their punches to conform to models that were in their heads. In that
    sense they were designers, whatever medium they used to create letters. \r\n\r\nI
    suspect that the 'rotation of the pen' in Dutch typefaces is an example of such
    interaction. You can get the vertical stress of the 'o' by changing the pen angle
    from the 'e'. But the question is, why would you bother to do this, which takes
    extra trouble in writing? It seems to me you would do this if you wanted to conform
    to a different design idea that you were trying to produce on paper.    \r\n\r\nAnd
    just for the record, when I write for publication, I thoroughly study the history
    of what I am writing about, unlike my guessing and rambling here on Typophile.
    \r\n"

  body: "[Bill] \u201C[\u2026] cutting their punches to conform to models that were
    in their heads [\u2026].\r\n\r\nWhen you adjust the contrast or the weight of
    storkes in the letters you design, do you do it because of a model in your head
    or because something is wrong in optical-grammatical terms and / or \u201Cgestural-atmospheric
    force\u201D terms? I believe Jenson and Griffo\u2019s innovations were the result
    of the characteristic featural play or exploration afforded by cutting punches.
    The \u2018end\u2019 of this is not evident at the start. The formalized result
    of exploration articulates a model only after play has resulted in something worth
    keeping.\r\n\r\nTo answer my own first question on this thread, perhaps when the
    changes are the result of the <em>characteristic</em> featural play or exploration
    <em>afforded by cutting punches</em>, writing becomes more typographic. But what
    keeps writing writing is writing."

- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>My guess, which may well be wrong, is that the letters such as
    the Shelly letters James showed us are constructed, not written. By that I mean
    that they look carefully built up of many strokes, not truly \u2019written\u2019,
    with a continuously moving front.</em>\r\n\r\nBut what you said was that you didn't
    see the influence of the split nib in the neo-classical roman types. The point
    that both Peter and I have made is that the influence is in the distinctive stroke
    contrast pattern. The fact that Shelley likely drew his c. 48pt letters (split
    nibs are notoriously difficult to use to write letters this large), or that Baskerville
    might have drawn rather than written his models for John Handy or used some mix
    of writing and drawing, doesn't alter the fact that what is employed in the shapes
    is a contrast pattern that is distinctive and based on that of the split nib pen.
    And for obvious reasons: the goal was to make harmonious letters at a range of
    sizes, all in keeping with the dominant text aesthetic (the social and economic
    background of which James has nicely encapsulated). So Shelley and other writing
    masters write at the sizes at which they can write, and draw at the sizes at which
    they can't write, but maintaining a harmony of style that is based on the characteristic
    contrast template -- to use Peter's term -- of the writing that is, after all,
    the more common."
  created: '2007-09-27 17:37:39'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Peter, there are two kinds of rotation:\r\n\r\nRotation between strokes,
    in which the angle of the hand is changed while the pen is lifted from the page.\r\n\r\nRotation
    within strokes, in which the angle of the hand is changed while the pen is moving
    in contact with the page.\r\n\r\nOne finds the first kind of rotation in all kinds
    of scripts, styles and periods, and this is what produces letters with variable
    axes. It is, for example, an essential component of the Byzantine cursive Greek
    miniscule, which always displays some variation in ductus.\r\n\r\nRotation within
    stroke is much less common, but this is the characteristic of mannerist writing
    with a broad nib that Noordzij has analysed so well in the work of Jan van den
    Velde. It is a virtuoso method: very difficult to learn but stunning when done
    well. So far as I can tell, it was not applied to 'roman' letterforms, though,
    and it's influence in type, beyond civilit\xE9, seems minimal and limited to some
    features in italics.\r\n\r\nThe kind of rotation you point to in Bringhurst's
    illustration is the first kind: rotation between strokes creating letters with
    varying ductus. But the ductus within the individual letters does not vary significantly.
    I suppose the implied slight rotation in the exit stroke of the e in some styles
    might be significant, but it could just as easily be a paratypographic feature
    of 'type design'."
  created: '2007-09-27 17:50:42'

    point.\r\n\r\nIt's worth noting that Baskerville's types are all pretty large
    by modern text standards. The neo-classical English book tends towards the monumental
    in its layout, which is why stylistic harmony between the different sizes of type
    was important."

  created: '2007-09-27 18:14:09'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Peter: <em>But in the typographical instatiations of the carolinian minuscule
    isn\u2019t the emulation of between-stroke rotation by the mannerists an innovation
    beyond their typographical predecessors?</em>\r\n\r\nI'm going to say yes, so
    long as one is only considering the instantiations of the caroline miniscule by
    those typographical predecessors. Obviously, if one also consider their instantiations
    of the Byzantine cursive miniscule, there is typographical precedence for the
    idea of between-stroke rotation. But the fact that this rotation only gets applied
    to roman letters at the time when rotation, both between-stroke and in-stroke,
    has become a feature of Latin script writing seems significant."
  created: '2007-09-27 18:51:20'
- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "John: <em>Rotation within stroke is much less common, </em>\r\n\r\nBut very
    common for finishing strokes. If you wish to create a terminal at a different
    angle to the angle of stress, you must rotate as you finish the stroke.\r\nWith
    a broad pen, if you incorporate removing one side of the pen before the other
    into the motion, you can create a perpendicular serif. But that is easier than
    rotating while holding the nib flat to the paper, because the rotation serves
    to lift one side of the nib off the paper. Dragging the dry corner of the nib,
    the build up of ink behind the nib is eased and tails off as it clings after the
    diminishing area of contact.\r\n\r\nSo that is how the top serif in this letter
    is formed, as the pen moves from right to left, rotates counter-clockwise, and
    the right side of the nib lifts off the paper, while the left corner eventually
    drags down vertically.\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/C_5429.jpg]"
  created: '2007-09-27 20:29:01'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: Yes. Lifting one side of the broad nib and writing with the corner is another
    virtuoso technique. As you note, Nick, in Latin script it is primarily used for
    certain kinds of terminals. In the <a href="http://www.columbia.edu/itc/mealac/pritchett/00xcallig/coloniallate/nastaliq/nastaliq2.jpg">nasta'liq
    </a>style of Arabic it is the cornerstone of the whole style.

  body: "Noordzij vs Harry Carter, the sequel. \r\n\r\nSo as I was saying, scripts
    like Bickham show the influence of the flexible pointed pen in a way that Baskerville
    does not. What do I mean by that?\r\n\r\nHere are a few characters from Bickham
    pro, but I suspect they are pretty true to the 'ductus' of the flexible pen:\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Bickham
    sample_3478.gif]\r\n\r\nI see here several things different form the broad pen
    type of writing, beyond greater contast. First, in the O we see that the pointed,
    flexible pen is able to get strokes in the same direction and same angle of different
    thicknesses. These are cleverly used a 'echo' shapes of different weights. Second,
    instead of the 'twisted ribbon' look of a broad pen changing direction, we have
    a gradual, sweeping swelling and diminishing of the stroke, and an ability to
    change angle without changing width (the left stroke of the N). . \r\n\r\nBy contrast,
    if we look at the typographic letters of Shelly, above, or at Baskerville, see
    more vertical stress, and greater contrast, but not the kinds violation of the
    broad pen as in the pointed pen script. Here is Baskerville:\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Baskerville
    Sample_5529.gif]\r\n\r\nWhat is going on here? Is there another influence, not
    from the flexible pointed pen? \r\n\r\nStay tuned for our next exciting adventure... "

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Was there any influence on Baskerville other than the pointed flexible pen,
    and the new things that were possible with it, and that it was conducive to?\r\n\r\nPeter
    writes above that the pointed flexible nib created a \"contrast manipulation paradigm
    that is fundamentally different in kind to that which came before.\"\r\n\r\nAnd
    John argues \"What you said was that you didn\u2019t see the influence of the
    split nib in the neo-classical roman types. The point that both Peter and I have
    made is that the influence is in the distinctive stroke contrast pattern.\"\r\n\r\nBut
    does the 'distinctive stroke contrast pattern' in Baskerville actually come from
    the flexible pointed nib, or are other sources equally or more important?\r\n\r\nAs
    Harry Carter argues in the earlier passage I quoted, the effort to find a geometrical
    'true' paradigm for the letters freed punch cutters to change their types from
    what the scribes had done. \r\n\r\nIs that kind of following a certain aesthetic
    ideal an important driving influence? Well the evidence? As James alludes to above
    the Romain du Roi type fifty years earlier already had some of the characteristics
    of Baskerville. And it was allegedly worked out using geometric models, as we
    can see here (image from Types of Typefaces):\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Romain
    du Roi_5473.gif]\r\n\r\nHowever, the pointed flexible nib had been introduced
    [[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nib_(pen)#Pointed_Nib|in the 17 century]], so perhaps
    this typeface was also just following the pointed nib pen, and the geometrical
    exercises were mere window dressing. \r\n\r\nThe problem with this theory can
    be seen in some of Albrecht D\xFCrer's drawings of letters:\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Durer
    C_3592.gif]\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Durer D_3897.gif]\r\n\r\nHere
    D\xFCrer does both vertically stressed characters and 'old style'. And he is quite
    conscious that the first, vertical stress letters violate the models of the broad
    pen. He writes in the description of the second 'C', \"and, as though made with
    a pen, let the descending stroke be heavier than the ascending.\"\r\n\r\nNote
    that the serifs are also more pointed than in any pen script, and they are also,
    in the H, I, etc, symmetrical. \r\n\r\nNow D\xFCrer did these engravings in 1525,
    so that is long before the flexible pointed nib was generally used. My own conclusion
    is that Harry Carter is right about there being a freedom to pursue new ideals
    not derived from writing. And those explorations eventually to the Romain du Roi
    and to Baskerville's work. \r\n\r\nAs a coda to this, let me return to the typographic
    engravings of Bickham. The fact that these drawings do not display very prominently
    some of the characteristic uses of the flexible pointed nib shown in the cursive
    writing indicates to me that these drawings are heavily influenced by type. Nevertheless,
    the  p in the top picture, for example, shows the presence of the flexible pointed
    pen in the fairly uniform, fine line arching into the stem above and below. However,
    in Baskerville, (at least in modern interpretations, and I think I see the same
    in the small sample above) there is more tapering, characteristic of earlier type,
    including Caslon, rather than writing. \r\n\r\nThese engravings of pen made roman
    letters are not so much writing as careful drawings that show the marked influence
    of type, and which try to work out ideas that go back to D\xFCrer, and probably
    earlier. This is not to deny that pointed nib makes a contribution by facilitating
    the exploration, but there is a lot besides writing going on. \r\n\r\nTomorrow:
    conclusions.   "

- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill, the fact that Baskerville's types display only <em>some</em> of the
    contrast patterns that may be produced with the split nib is not an indication
    that the split nib, via the lettering of the contemporary writing masters -- including
    Baskerville himself! -- is not the primary influence. One also doesn't see the
    kind of parallel strokes of different contrast in the formal roman letters written
    by those writing masters, as seen in the illustrations I provided above, only
    in their cursive 'script' styles. The reason you don't see it in the roman letters
    is that such parallel strokes are not a part of the letterform.\r\n\r\nThe fact
    remains that you can't produce Baserville's letters using any other writing implement
    but a split nib. The fact remains that Baskerville was a master of writing with
    the split nib. The fact remains that English writing masters were producing roman
    letters in his style several decades before his first types were cut. The fact
    remains, that other than blackletter, all the styles produced by these writing
    masters were based on the dynamics of the split nib.\r\n\r\nBy the way, the example
    of large letters produced by George Shelley, presented by Beatrice Warde in her
    <em>Monotype Recorder</em> article on Baskerville and reproduced by James above,
    is dated 1715. Today I found an example of much smaller roman letters written
    by Shelley (about the size of the main body type of Baskerville's Milton) in 1709.
    If you didn't realise it was written and not type, and someone asked you to name
    the typeface, you would say Baskerville. I'll post a photo shortly.\r\n\r\n*   *
    \  *\r\n\r\nIf you are going to introduce the Roman du Roi, then I would say that
    we have to start paying attention to French writing masters as well as English
    ones. Just as you can't ignore what English writing masters were producing in
    the decades before Baskerville's types, you can't triumphantly point to the precedence
    of the Roman du Roi without taking into account what French writing masters were
    producing in the decades before it was devised. Thankfully, we happen to have
    one of the the foremost authorities on the Roman du Roi reading this discussion.
    Thankfully, also, I happen to have his book. Page 32 shows 'Devises pour les tapisseries
    du Roi, 1668.... la calligraphie est vraisemblablement de Jarry'. Do I need to
    say that it shows essentially the style of the Roman du Roi type, written with
    a split nib pen, thirty years before the cutting of the typeface?\r\n\r\n*   *
    \  *\r\n\r\nPace Harry Carter, D\xFCrer's constructed letters, like the numerous
    other renaissance exercises in attempting to devise or prove a geometric basis
    for roman capitals, exist entirely outside the mainstream of text production.
    They have nothing to do with the making of text, either in manuscript or in type.
    \r\n\r\n<em>As Harry Carter argues in the earlier passage I quoted, the effort
    to find a geometrical \u2019true\u2019 paradigm for the letters freed punch cutters
    to change their types from what the scribes had done.</em>\r\n\r\nIt's a nice
    idea, but I don't think there is visual evidence to back it up. Punchcutters are
    providing types to produce text, within text cultures. The theoretical and conceptual
    exercises of artists, accountants, and varied other people who dabbled in the
    geometric construction of letters seems to me to have had remarkably little influence
    on the development of typographic letters. This isn't surprising, because such
    exercises had remarkably little impact on the contemporary text culture.\r\n\r\nWhat
    D\xFCrer and other letter-constructors were trying to do was to establish a geometric
    basis for the classical roman capitals. That is, they were taking something that
    already existed, and trying to map it to various kinds of geometries to see how
    well it fit and to iron out suspected non-rational inconsistencies. I think the
    committee and draughtmen responsible for the famous engraved Roman du Roi plates
    were engaged in the same kind of exercise: taking the kind of letters being written
    by master scribes like Nicolas Jarry -- employed in the service of the same king
    as the makers of the Roman du Roi --, fitting them within a grid, and imposing
    geometries on the construction of details. In the process, they rationalised many
    aspects of the construction. What is interesting, of course, and oft remarked
    on, is that Grandjean's actual type does not follow these engraved plates. It
    is, in fact, very close to Jarry's 'calligraphie'. Why? <em>Because that's the
    contemporary text culture.</em>"
