- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">Are the norm-violations \u2018essential,\u2019 and \u2018genotypical\u2019?
    \r\n\r\nI think 'yes'. \r\n\r\nI would have to get a broad pen and try to write
    over Garamond to test it out properly. But I don't see how you can get a wider
    vertical arch on the D than the stem of the D by translation, and no rotation
    or expansion. You will have to do rotation, at the very least, and then the question
    would be, why are you doing it, when they didn't before? Optically a fatter arch
    balances better with a vertical stem. Why ignore that as a driving factor? It
    also ends up with more even color as a result, which, carried through systematically,
    is better for readability. \r\n\r\nBut the spacing issues don't have to do with
    the 'ductus' anyway. The point is, Noordzij's categories of 'ductus' aren't sufficient
    to capture important features of the development of type design. Visual features
    concerning optical balance, even color, spacing and rhythm are different variables,
    and are critical.\r\n\r\nNoordzij does have interesting insights into optical
    effects, but he doesn't integrate these into his history, so far as I remember. "
  created: '2007-10-08 16:01:57'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, in the Garamond lower case you show above, the <em>pattern</em> of
    contrast is massively set by the translation <em>paradigm</em>. This does not
    depend on each and every characteristic of the Garamond type being executable
    to the finest detail by the tool it's contrast <em>type</em> derives from.\r\n\r\nThe
    norm violations are incidental and \u2014 I think \u2014 progressive, i.e., in
    the direction of optimizing affordances, not relative \u2014 oppositionally \u2014
    to writing, but inside the domain of the specific letter set they were creating.\r\n\r\nWriting
    set the spacing paradigm of rhythmic coherence between the interior and exterior
    forms of letters within words, and a break from this in the spaces between the
    words. Type designers devised their own ways of consolidating this as well.\r\n\r\nIf
    the type designers asked themselves anything, it was probably: how can I keep
    spacing rhythmic in the new environment of rectangular sorts. What do I need to
    do to proportions and widths, and, with widths also \u2014 and in consequence
    \u2014 weights?"

  created: '2007-10-08 16:45:05'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "The point is, Peter, the violations are systematic, and being pushed by different
    goals, or a different 'paradigm', if you will. In that sense, yes, they are changing
    the paradigm in a fundamental and systematic way.\r\n\r\nThe broad pen remains
    an \"underlying force\" (van Krimpen) in Garamond, but is contradicted by another
    force: the greater freedom of the punch cutter to please the reading eye more
    than the moving broad pen was able to. I never said the pen wasn't a force, just
    that there were other forces pushing away from its lines. You say that the changes
    are \"critical\", \"strategic\", but \"non-essential\". I think you are getting
    into a distinction without a difference here. If they modify and contradict the
    rules of the pen in a systematic way, I don't see how they can be anything but
    an essential change. \r\n\r\nAnother way you can look at this is that there are
    two forces or paradigms at work: one the pattern set by the pen, and the other
    what pleases the reading eye. Noordzij rightly highlights the importance of one,
    but not the other, in the historical evolution of type design. You need both to
    get the full picture, I think. \r\n\r\nAnd later on in history you get a desire
    to meet some aesthetic standards that even make text type less readable. Even
    there it is those aesthetic ideals, and not the pen alone that is driving the
    process. Eg making the lower case wider, and the caps more even width, doesn't
    come from the pen. "
  created: '2007-10-08 17:28:25'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>Thus Baskerville didn\u2019t just violate the broad pen, he violated
    it in a way that is characteristic of the pointed, flexible pen.</em>\r\n\r\nIt
    seems to me that saying that Baskerville 'violated the broad pen' implies a relationship
    to that implement that is absent not only from Baskerville's own work, as both
    a writing master and as a typographer/printer, but also absent -- with the exception
    of blackletter -- from the contemporary text creation culture. It makes almost
    as little sense as saying that he violated the Chinese brush or the Burmese stylus:
    the broad nib as a tool for the creation of text was alien to his culture and
    hadn't been widely used since before Baskerville's birth. And even if you want
    to say that he violated the latent, inherited translation stroke pattern in typographic
    letters, that still implies a relationship that I think is contradicted by the
    evidence.\r\n\r\nNow I think we need to look at what happens when a script is
    reduced to typography. Gerald already commented on some implications of this,
    including the typical loss of regional scripts (although this had already begun
    in the manuscript culture as early as Charlemagne's time, and some regional scripts
    either survived or were later revived, e.g. the Irish insular hand). But there
    is a more general case to be observed, which is that the norm for typographic
    letters derives from the canons of the contemporary formal book hand, but in the
    new medium undergoes a secondary canonisation. That is, the normative manuscript
    hand of a particular text culture becomes the normative typographic letter of
    <em>subsequent</em> text cultures. So in renaissance Italy, the humanist formal
    book hand and later the cursive hand become the normative forms of humanist typography,
    which eventually becomes <em>the</em> normative typographic form of Latin letters,
    supplanting previous styles such as the various blackletter and regional hands
    and admitting new styles derived from contemporary writing, such as Civilit\xE9,
    only as supplementary fashions. So the process of type design becomes the process
    of creating these normative letterforms in new ways, with different proportions,
    contrast and detailing, rather than the process of creating new styles of writing.
    But in the meantime, new styles of writing are being invented by writing masters
    and professional scribes, who are producing significant quantities of text in
    various kinds of documents, who are teaching new styles of fashionable handwriting
    to both children and adults, and who are publishing copy books and exemplars,
    all in response to broader developments in intellectual and cultural life. Meanwhile,
    in comparison, the norms of typographic letterforms develop hardly at all: they
    are locked in their original Italian humanist form. To the type designer and typographic
    historian, there is a rich development in the treatment of proportions, weights,
    serif treatment, etc. and the important development of the canon of typographic
    articulation through supplementary styles, italics, smallcaps, etc. But compared
    to the range of different writing styles developed during the same period, Latin
    typography remains largely static and the basic underlying forms of the letters
    remains frozen and continues to be frozen today. Indeed, we now associate the
    normative forms not only of our typography but of our alphabet with these particular
    historical shapes. We admit some variation in the forms of e.g. a and g, but most
    of the rest of the historical and regional variety of the Latin script is unknown
    to most people.\r\n\r\nWhat happens in the 18th century is that these typographic
    norms are re-intepreted in light of the principle tool of contemporary writing
    and its distinctive stroke pattern. This is done by writing masters first, and
    only some decades later by a 'type designer'. In stylistic terms, everything Baskerville
    needed to create his types is already there in Shelley's exemplars of 1709 and
    1715. By the 1750s, this kind of 'typographic writing', i.e. the normative typographic
    letterforms as written with a pen, has become part of the standard repertoire
    of the writing masters, and taking the next step of making it into a typeface
    is largely a financial and manufacturing issue. There are particular design considerations
    relating to the size of individual fonts, but that has been the case in typography
    since at least Griffo's time: the style of the new types was developed by writing
    them, as the documentary evidence shows. This may be a unique phenomenon in the
    history of typography: the development of a significant new style within the overall
    canon of typographic letterforms, i.e. not a new style of writing but a new way
    of making typographic shapes in a contemporary way, developed independently of
    the manufacture of type (something similar is happening in France at around the
    same time, but can probably be considered part of the same phenomenon, especially
    if one takes into account Fournier's enthusiasm for Baskerville's types)."
  created: '2007-10-08 17:36:16'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>If they modify and contradict the rules of the pen in a systematic
    way, I don\u2019t see how they can be anything but an essential change.</em>\r\n\r\nBecause
    they dont contradict the rules of the <em>stroke?</em> I think Peter, and almost
    certainly Noordzij, consider the stroke pattern as the essential characteristic,
    and the various parachirographic tactics that modify weight and otherwise adjust
    the arrangement and relationship of strokes do not change the paradigm established
    by the stroke pattern. So, for instance, Garamond's D includes certain adjustments
    to stroke weight that contradict the rules (characteristics) of the broad nib
    pen, but the strokes remain describable in terms of translation and rotation (the
    latter showing the mannerist influence)."
  created: '2007-10-08 17:51:25'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">saying that Baskerville \u2019violated the broad pen\u2019 implies a relationship
    to that implement that is absent not only from Baskerville\u2019s own work, as
    both a writing master and as a typographer/printer, bit also absent \u2014 with
    the exception of blackletter \u2014 from the contemporary text creation culture.\r\n\r\nThat
    is a question-begging argument, as it assumes what is under question. You are
    assuming that 'text creation culture' excludes printing and printer's types, whose
    influence is under question. But printing is text creation also. People of the
    day, including Baskerville, read a lot of text in newspapers and magazines and
    books--probably a lot more than hand writing--and these printers types followed
    the old model, influenced by the broad pen. That old model had been fundamentally
    modified for the eye, as I have been arguing with Peter, but none the less the
    broad pen was there as an underlying force. "
  created: '2007-10-08 18:32:00'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>You are assuming that \u2019text creation culture\u2019 excludes
    printing and printer\u2019s types...</em>\r\n\r\nNo, I'm not assuming that at
    all. I began my part in this discussion about Baskerville by insisting that printer's
    types be seen in the larger context of text creation that, in cultures with significant
    manuscript document production, has certain observable characteristics and influences
    that argue against the accuracy of any typocentric history. This isn't a statement
    only about 18th century England, but what happened there is particularly interesting
    because the model for the new types is so clearly established in a different medium,
    writing, prior to being implemented in type, and we have such good documentary
    evidence of this because of the social and economic importance of writing and
    the production so many copy books and exemplars.\r\n\r\nYes, the broad pen, i.e.
    the translation stroke pattern, is there in the imported printing types and in
    Caslon's types (although I think you did an admirable job, earlier in this discussion,
    of demonstrating influences of neo-classicism in some of Caslon's forms, whether
    that was your intention or not), but my point made earlier was that this left
    typography increasingly at odds with and out of step with the aesthetics of the
    text creation culture dominated by written, engraved and inscribed forms that
    were based on very different stroke patterns. And the strongest evidence of this
    domination is that there is, during half a century or more, no counter influence
    of these old fashioned types on any other area of text creation. That seems to
    me very significant.\r\n\r\nSo when you say that Baskerville 'violated the broad
    pen', i.e. broke with the translation stroke pattern, I think you are still imposing
    a typocentric view that doesn't fit with the evidence. The evidence suggests that
    Baskerville spent his whole life writing with a flexible split nib steel pen,
    that he became a writing master and practitioner of the various styles of writing
    and lettering associated with that pen, and that he later had two of these styles
    cut in type by John Handy. In order to 'violate the broad pen' he would have had
    to have had some engagement with the broad pen, and I just don't see it and I
    don't count reading books set in Caslon's types to constitute such an engagement.\r\n\r\nYou
    could argue reasonably, I think, that the first person or persons to write the
    normative typographic forms of Latin letters with a split nib 'violated the broad
    pen'. But that wasn't Baskerville, and you are unjustifiably privileging the act
    of cutting those letters in metal if you imply that Baskerville or Handy's achievement
    constitutes such violation but not George Shelley's, <em>four decades earlier.</em>\r\n\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-09 00:08:32'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, it doesn't matter to my claim that the \u2018norm violations\u2019
    are systematic or dramatic, as with the oblique stroke in the garamond \u2018z\u2018.
    To again use the biological terminological apparatus, the normviolations you described
    exist at the level of phenotypical elaboration and not at the level of genotypical
    kind. That's why I called them incidental rather than essential, and added that
    incidental and non-essential doesn't mean non-important. I meant that in both
    an historical and a 'robustness of performance\u2019 sense. The genotypical kind
    exists, moreover, at a set level, not individual sorts. \r\n\r\nAnd: what are
    those <em>different</em> goals? A goal, moreover, is not a paradigm, and neither
    is the pressure to please the eye or meet it\u2019s needs. Changing goals or adding
    a new one is not the same as changing paradigms."
  created: '2007-10-09 02:05:53'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Peter, Popper has a whole chapter hammering 'essentialism'--the method of
    searching for definitions to capture essences of something. This approach is based
    on the Aristotelian concepts of essence and accident, and has been abandoned by
    modern science. Poppers argues, rightly I think, that it predictably decays into
    verbal disputes. So I don't want to argue about essences, which I think is fruitless.
    \r\n\r\nMy basic point here is that type designers systematically altered hand
    writing in ways to please the eye. And those alterations are missing in Noordzij's
    account of the history, and so the account in my eyes omits one important dimension
    of the history of type design, a dimension not ruled by the stroke of the pen. "
  created: '2007-10-09 03:34:33'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Don't worry Bill, I'm neither an essentialist nor an Aristotelian.\r\n\r\nMy
    basic point has been that the norm violations you described exist at the level
    of phenotypical elaboration and not at the level of genotypical kind. And I am
    wary about how you want to account for them / what value or \u2018meaning\u2019
    you want to ascribe to them.\r\n\r\nI could add as a concession that the pattern
    of norm violations in for example Garamond, while not changing essentially or
    fundamentally the foundational paradigm for apportioning contrast in the stroke,
    can become paradigmatic for the culture they happen in.\r\n\r\nIn general I want
    to hold out an openess to this: Noordzij's account accommodates more than he details,
    and he details more than you give him credit for, even in the article you introduced."
  created: '2007-10-09 04:24:16'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201Csystematically altered hand writing in ways to please the eye\u201D
    [Bill]\r\n\r\nI would put it differently.\r\n\r\nType designers systematically
    introduced alterations to what the first transpositions of writing into type produced.
    And they did so along several axis. \r\n\r\nThe first axis, I'll call the <em>optical-grammatical
    problem-solving</em> axis.\r\nIt's not problems reading scribal writing that provoked
    the alterations, it's things that appear when the small variations introduced
    by the human hand and physical tool are resolved into a workable amount of  standardized
    and co-ordinated widths, standardized heights (x-height, cap height, ascender
    height, descender depth) and standardized stroke proportions. This sets up a type-internal
    dynamic. And I think that's what you miss in Noordzij. The \u2018o\u2019 overshoot
    problem is of this sort, as is the incremental widening across the board of certain
    strokes.\r\n\r\nSmall variation in pressure, rotation, proportion is a thing of
    the hand, and over the space of a text produces a consistent texture. Constraining
    variations to absolute values \u2014 which the new technology presupposes \u2014
    allows optical issues of uneven colour and optical irregularities \u2014 <em>which
    aren't spontaneously evoked in an environment of random, small-incremental variation</em>
    \u2014 to assert themselves or obtrude.\r\n\r\n(The second axis I've tried to
    call the <em>gestural-atmospheric-force inflection</em> axis.)\r\n\r\nNorm-violations
    along the optical-grammatical problem-solving axis aren\u2019t generally stroke-paradigm
    changing, but they can (help) push the paradigm to a breaking point.\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-09 16:05:15'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "An afterthought: I think Noordzij's concern is to not have a focus on what
    I refer to above as \u2018type-internal dynamics\u2019 overwhelm  or eclipse a
    larger, deeper-level and generally untold story, such as he seeks to narrate with
    his matrixes of letters in the RIDT essay I posted."
  created: '2007-10-09 18:13:35'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Peter, I like your analysis of how the inherent consistency of type--the
    same 'n' etc. everywhere--causes irregularities to protrude, which might otherwise
    be submerged in the general variability of hand writing. And thus the producers
    of type, as opposed to writing, needed to deal with details even more exactly
    than the scribes did. The care with spacing (in justification of matrices) is
    probably also a consequence. \r\n\r\nThe regularity of type may also may make
    the dimension of evenness or unevenness of color stand out. To deal with it, by
    the time of Garamond, anyway, we have a *contradiction* of the broad pen in systematic
    ways, including, as I mentioned, the thinning of 'thick' diagonals and the expansion
    of vertical rounds compared to stems. But it goes way beyond this, and includes
    the weight of terminals, the shapes of letters, their relative widths etc.  \r\n\r\nYou
    won't see these kinds of modification of vertical arches or thinner diagonals
    on the Trajan Column, or in hand writing with the broad pen, so far as I know.
    How you want to label this designing for the eye--'essential' to type 'genotypical'
    or whatever--doesn't matter. I do know that it is type-specific, systematic, and
    not derived from the broad pen. For type design, it is an equally or perhaps even
    more important part of the story as the that story of original basis of the letters
    in pen-drawn shapes, a story that Noordzij has shined new light on.  "

  created: '2007-10-09 18:59:44'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>To deal with it, by the time of Garamond, anyway, we have a *contradiction*
    of the broad pen in systematic ways...\r\n\r\nMy basic point here is that type
    designers systematically altered hand writing in ways to please the eye. And those
    alterations are missing in Noordzij\u2019s account of the history...</em>\r\n\r\nBut
    not, I think, from his account of the stroke. The alterations take place within
    the logic of the stroke model, even as they sometimes transcend what the actual
    pen is capable of in a single stroke or in a consistent ductus (and bear in mind
    that consistent ductus is a feature only of some writing systems and styles).
    Consider what Peter identifies as a 'dramatic' example of such alteration, the
    contrast pattern of the Garamond z, which becomes the normative pattern for the
    typographic letter: clearly the implied ductus of the strokes is inconsistent
    with the other letters, <em>but the stroke model is still analysable in terms
    of translation.</em> Likewise the other alterations in renaissance types that
    you have identified: they transcend the pen by extending the logic of the pen,
    not, as you suggest, by contradicting it. They are, to use a term Peter introduced
    on the ATypI discussion list, 'parachirographic'."
  created: '2007-10-09 20:06:45'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">the logic of the pen\r\n\r\nThe logic of which pen? If we are talking about
    one broad pen, with a fixed width held at a constant angle--which I gather is
    the story through Garamond--then what I have described are contradictions to that
    pen. \r\n\r\nSure, if you change to a different pen of different width when you
    do a diagonal, or somehow rotate the pen at weird angles while writing the letter--you
    might get those uniform or thinner diagonals, though probably if you kept the
    pen on the paper it would mess up the stroke at vertexes while you rotate. And
    you'd have to switch or rotate the pen to get the wider arches too. Switching
    pens for heavier caps makes some sense, but not in the middle of a lower case
    letter, I think. \r\n\r\nA theoretical pen is irrelevant to causation in history,
    because it didn't exist and it didn't drive the historical development. \r\n\r\nI
    find Peter's analysis persuasive--that the inherent uniformity of repeating the
    same letter forms pushed type creators to make the changes I have written about.
    That way they were able to achieve better rhythm in letter widths and spacing,
    and more uniform color, enhancing easy and comfortable reading. And by the way
    not all were able to do it. As Harry Carter illustrates, W. de Spira's roman type
    failed to achieve that good typographic rhythm, and Jenson succeeded. And he thereby
    became a model for Griffo. There are typographic solutions to typographic problems,
    and these are not trivial, and not simply derivative from writing.  \r\n\r\nEarlier
    in this thread, having before my mind's eye Noordzij's theoretical pen--that can
    do translation, rotation, and expansion--caused me not to see Baskerville's arches
    as guided by the ductus of the pointed pen. The pointed pen is, however, a real
    pen and doesn't act the same way in down stroke, upstroke, and moving sideways,
    and so is decisively different from that theoretical pen. I only really fully
    understood that then when I picked up a pointed pen and wrote with it. Now I think
    you are making a different mistake, but due to the same cause--assuming a theoretical
    pen that didn't exist and didn't influence history.\r\n\r\nThe changes in pens
    did influence letter forms, as Noordzij explains. But other forces also influenced
    the evolution of type. "
  created: '2007-10-09 21:29:09'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill, I'm talking about the translation stroke pattern, in which the angle
    of the moving front -- the broad nib -- is constant throughout the stroke. Note:
    not necessarily constant across every stroke in every letter, but within each
    individual stroke. And my point is that where e.g. Garamond's letterforms differ
    from the scribal model on which the roman types were based they do so in ways
    that are consistent with the translation stroke pattern. This is why I say they
    are transcending the pen, not contradicting it: the logic of the stroke pattern
    is extended and applied independently of the model, but the strokes remain informed
    by the translation pattern, not by some other pattern.\r\n\r\n<em>A theoretical
    pen is irrelevant to causation in history, because it didn\u2019t exist and it
    didn\u2019t drive the historical development.</em>\r\n\r\nWell a theoretical pen
    exists as soon as one thinks of it, so you can't say it did or didn't exist without
    knowing how the punchcutters thought about letterforms. But I don't think a theoretical
    pen, i.e. a theoretical understanding of the moving front such as Noordzij has
    developed, is necessary to applying the stroke pattern of particular writing implements
    systematically and independently of that pen. Type designers do this all the time,
    and stonecutters and woodblock cutters have been doing it much longer: you get
    a feel for a stroke model and how to apply it and, yes, also how to manipulate
    and transcend it.\r\n\r\n<em>There are typographic solutions to typographic problems,
    and these are not trivial, and not simply derivative from writing.</em>\r\n\r\nAbsolutely.
    In this I completely agree with you, and much earlier in this discussion I referred
    to some of those specifically typographic problems that are associated with scale.
    But when we are talking about the development of styles of type and typography
    -- as we are when we start talking about what distinguishes Baskerville's types
    from Caslon's --, then we need to incorporate in our view a much broader text
    creation culture, and not limit ourselves to 'type history'."

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "John, it's not just adjustments for small size. The Gros Canon of Garamond,
    above is pretty big. Canon is 48 pt, so I supposed 'Gros' Canon is bigger, a display
    size. And in addition to not following rules of any real broad pen of fixed width,
    many strokes also violate the rule of sticking to translation. The bottom of the
    stems in Garamond often widen out symmetrically before reaching the bracketing
    of the serif; that broadening is an expansion, violating the fixed width of a
    pen. \r\n\r\nI haven't been writing here only about changes in style, but also
    in readability. But in any case Garamond, it seems to me, is a significantly different
    style from humanist hand writing. If you put the Gros Canon and your post of humanist
    hand writing side by side, I think most typophiles would say that there is a marked
    stylistic difference. And that difference in style was doesn't seem to have been
    driven by changes in hand writing. "
  created: '2007-10-10 18:00:19'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, you are still mixing up what the pen can do with the contrast styling
    paradigm that is native to it and it offers. The contrast type native to the broad-nibbed
    pen becomes the basis of a paraphrase of it in the typographical medium. The paraphrase
    is not exact. But the contrast styling paradigm visibly and emphatically persists
    there \u2014 right through a whole series of beyond-paraphrastic (para-paraphrastic)
    adjustments \u2014 before it gets replaced. The beyond-paraphrastic adjustments
    don't really care about what the pen can or cannot do, though they are mindful
    of what it\u2019s done. The systematicity of the ajustments can only be seen from
    the perspective of the scheme.\r\n\r\nYou want to know what drives them. You want
    to say: concerns about readability. I tried to say the readability of writing
    in any normal everyday experiential sense was not a / the problem. I've tried
    to circumscribe \u2018what drive\u2019 in terms of optical-grammatic pressures
    and gestural-atmospheric likes. And, my thinking is that, as a kind of epiphenomenal
    gift, affordances were consolidated or enhanced. Where I say affordances, you
    might want to substitute your 'readability.' And that is your prerogative. By
    my sense is that the term is inadequate as an historical explanatory concept."
  created: '2007-10-10 19:38:27'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Peter, I have always acknowledged that humanist formal writing is the starting
    point of type, and has remained an \"unlying force\" in type--even in Bodoni etc.
    \r\n\r\nWhat drives changes? I have said I like your ideas that irregularities
    that pass in handwriting will become problematic in type. \r\n\r\nWhether the
    creators of type thought 'that doesn't look right' or 'that is distracting' or
    'that isn't so smooth for reading' or whatever, I don't know. I think there is
    an intuitive reaction of 'rightness' that reflects how our visual apparatus works,
    and that was at work in their minds, as it is in ours. \r\n\r\nIt think also that
    aesthetic fashions were huge influences. I would guess that these drove both the
    adoption of the pointed, flexible pen nib, and the development of high contrast
    type--the same forces behind both, though the particular line of the pointed pen
    also influenced Baskerville.  "

- author:
    name: Giampa
  body: "Nick\"\u2013\u2013\"But surely the Americans can\u2019t be faulted for developing
    their own type culture and types, especially the Scotch romans of such as the
    Bruce foundry in the mid 19th century.\r\n\r\nSurely you jest, \"Scotch American\"?
    I'll have what you are having, pour me a glass!\r\n\r\nI am in agreement with
    Norbert Florendo\u2013\u2013\"Linotype refers to Scotch Roman designs first cut
    by Englishman Richard Austin and cast by the Scottish typefounder Alexander Wilson
    and Son in Glasgow.\"\r\n\r\nMaybe I have sadly lived under delusions but am comfortable
    that you, as usual, will enjoy setting me straight. I thank now before I forget.\r\n\r\nKidding
    aside\u2013\u2013\r\n\r\nI am under the impression that you think \"American superior
    type culture\" displaced Caslon. Perhaps you may entertain the following suggestions.\r\n\r\nAmerican
    founders were \"non to pleased with  the longevity of Caslon's popularity\". They
    wanted to impose an \"import protection act\". The problem was that lead, and
    lead products were tariff free. The weasel factor came into play, the founders
    claimed that \"Caslon types were not lead\", that is why their type was so hard,
    not to mention, superior to American type products, therefore, \"subject to tariff\".
    In order for the Caslon Foundery to dispute this they had to demonstrate that
    their alloy was mainly composed of lead and \"disclose the alloy\"\r\n\r\nNaturally
    the Caslon Foundery's alloy was a closely guarded secret.\r\n\r\nSo you see, it
    was a win win for the American founderies. One way they would discover the alloy,
    the other they would have a greater financial advantage in price for inferior
    products.\r\n\r\nEventually Caslon tried to get around this by licensing the typeface
    supplying punches for strikes. In my day, ATF was able to readily supply an exact
    duplicate from these strikes, not to mention that eventually the punches in one
    size were lent to the Lanston Monotype Machine Company of Philadelphia.\r\n\r\nRemember,
    you heard it here first.\r\n\r\nGiampa"
  created: '2007-10-11 04:39:05'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>And in addition to not following rules of any real broad pen of
    fixed width, many strokes also violate the rule of sticking to translation. The
    bottom of the stems in Garamond often widen out symmetrically before reaching
    the bracketing of the serif; that broadening is an expansion, violating the fixed
    width of a pen.</em>\r\n\r\nNo, that's not expansion, that's the typical pattern
    of writing formal roman letters with a broad nib (or a brush for that matter:
    you see the same thing in the Trajan letter). What it indicates is a double stroke:
    the pen comes down and then out towards one serif, then is lifted and placed back
    on the stem and brought down and out towards the other serif. In the process,
    it is typical for the stem to flare, since the transition to the serif is not
    sharp. Pretty much any calligraphy text book will illustrate this.\r\n\r\nNot
    only is this flaring not indicative of expansion, with an expansion tool you typically
    get a much more abrupt transition to the serif, because you are releasing pressure
    at the bottom of the stem. Hence the flat serifs in most Didot and Bodoni revivals.\r\n\r\n<em>I
    have said I like [Peter's] ideas that irregularities that pass in handwriting
    will become problematic in type.</em>\r\n\r\nYes, I like this too, and it makes
    a lot of sense: writing is a more forgiving medium precisely because it is inconsistent
    so 'mistakes' are not repeated every time a letter is made. When you 'write with
    prefabricated letters' to use Noordzij's phrase, you want them to be at their
    best.\r\n\r\nYour point about adjustments in larger sizes of type is well made,
    but I think we find a similar approach to 'optical correction' in larger sizes
    of drawn letters too, e.g. in mediaeval versal letters. Writing is very size specific
    in that most styles are based on a relationship of letter proportion to the size
    of the writing implement. [This is explicit in the teaching of the traditional
    Arabic styles: everything is related to the width of the reed nib.] So we can
    expect that there will be more adjustment of letterforms based on writing styles
    such as the Italian humanist book hand at sizes below and above those normally
    written in that style by the scribe. And I think this is observable: at smaller
    sizes, adjustments are made to improve legibility of text, and at larger sizes
    adjustments are made to improve the balance or beauty of individual letters, such
    as those seen in Garamond's gros canon. The closer you are to the typical size
    of the written style, the less important either kind of adjustment is going to
    be, because the writing was tailored to that size to begin with."

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "John, if you allow built-up letters, then any shape can be done by the broad
    pen.\r\n\r\nTheoretically, the humanist script could have been written the way
    you say, lifting the pen for the bottom right serif, and starting a new stroke
    from above in the stem. But it doesn't seem to have been done this way in reality.
    Instead, the pen is moved to the left for a shorter left serif, and then moved
    directly across to the right in a straight line, for a longer right serif. \r\n\r\nThis
    is way of making serifs is reflected in the humanist script you posted on 23 September,
    and every one I've seen, though I've only seen samples here and there. I did see
    an actual page of Alcuin's magnificent Carolingian Miniscule recently at an exhibition
    at the Smithsonian here, and it was done this way. \r\n\r\nThis asymmetrical shape
    of humanist writing is shown schematically on p. 97 of Gerrit Noordzij's Letterletter.
    He notes the difference between the way the humanist script was written and the
    more symmetric serifs of early type. Noordzij refers to Jenson's Roman as having
    the symmetrical serifs, which I think is a mistake--it's Griffo who introduced
    the symmetry. If you want to see a clear digital example of following the broad
    pen way of doing serifs, see Trinite, at TEFF.nl \r\n\r\nGriffo's symmetrical
    serifs in my view are typographic rather than chirographic--following what the
    eye wants, not the convenience of the hand. The tapering that Garamond does is
    also, I would think, not the way the hand would do it with a broad pen, even if
    the letter were stroked this way. His tapers start relatively far up the stem,
    not near the bottom. To my eyes they are carefully designed for even color, and
    for aesthetic reasons. The branch joining the n, h, etc adds weight, and the stems
    are thinner there. The slight additional weight on the lower stem evens out the
    look. \r\n\r\nIf Garamond were already influenced by a more flexible pen, you
    would have an argument for his shapes being influenced by writing. But with the
    broad pen and humanist scripts, I don't think the argument is cogent. \r\n\r\nNoordzij
    notes that symmetrical serifs are not chirographic, and attempts to account for
    this. He says that \"Jenson could have copied this [assymetrical lozenge] shape
    faithfully in his punches, but the extra work would not have paid. ...rectangular
    footings are much more easily cut than lozenges.\" This argument doesn't hold
    water, because in fact Jenson did serifs that more resemble humanist writing,
    and then Griffo moved further away.  \r\n\r\nHe goes on to note other differences
    of type from writing but argues to minimize or dismiss them. John, you, being
    more holy than Pope Gerrit, even deny the differences. But that denial doesn't
    stand up to the reality of the record, I think. The influences were not simply
    the hand, but also the eye and ideals of aesthetics and ease of reading. \r\n\r\nNoordzij
    is keen to controvert \"the time when everybody was eager to hear stories about
    a typography which could be independent of handwriting.\" He is right of course
    about the strong influence of the pen, and his important contributions have established
    that. But to say that other considerations are not key parts of the story is to
    go to the other extreme. That is understandable in a pioneering and corrective
    effort, but also wrong. In my view there is a dialectic between the hand and the
    eye in the development of type, and the eye often leads, and sometimes contradicts
    what the hand would write. \r\n \r\n\r\n\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-12 16:08:48'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201Cthere is a dialectic between the hand and the eye in the development
    of type\u201D [Bill]\r\n\r\nThis converation exists also in the developement of
    scribal writing, and with equal force: if the pen is the hammer, the eye is the
    anvil in paleographic change.\r\n\r\nHow does the eye lead? How does this dialectic
    proceed? Only by the eye reacting. And it doesn't react in type design to what
    the hand might write, but what a specific paraphrase of such writing in a new
    medium puts in place or presents us with. That's why I like to think of the modifications
    as para-paraphrastic, rather than anti- or non- or contra-chirographic: the fundamental
    thing is that the relation of type to writing is foundationally or originally
    that of paraphrase. And that paraphrastic relationship is never not in some way
    always ever there.\r\n\r\nI think what John might be highlighting, Bill, is that
    not only does the pen provide the foundational contrast template, but it also
    can provide a partial grammer of the kinds of modifications or adjustments that
    might be introduced. What John is describing is shown by Noordzij in <em>Letterletter</em>
    on page 144 in relation to the Burgundian Bastarda."
  created: '2007-10-12 18:14:47'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "I said: \u201CI like to think of the modifications as para-paraphrastic,
    rather than anti- or non- or contra-chirographic.\u201D\r\n\r\nLet me illustrate
    this with the oblique stroke in the z.\r\n\r\nA direct paraphrase of writing puts
    in place a thin oblique stroke in the z. At a certain point in type this oblique
    is brought into harmony with other obliques inclined in the opposite direction
    in the alphabet. This is done because it's lightness is obtrusive. The adjustment
    is in the direction of what a direct paraphrase of writing introduces in other
    key obliques. So it is only anti-chirographic in a very limited sense.\r\n\r\nThe
    source is other obliques. I don't know if there is a historical precedent in writing.
    The <em>direction</em> of the change is not anti-chirographic, though <em>structurally</em>
    the norm violation is dramatic when seen against the translational contrast paradigm."
  created: '2007-10-12 18:46:03'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill, the two-stroke serif formation I described is typical of writing formal
    capitals with a broad tool. It is the method Catich describes for the classical
    roman capitals painted with a brush. It is what every calligraphy text book teaches
    for creating this kind of serif. As you observe, the serif in the typical humanist
    miniscule hand is different, and that type makers adopted the symetrical serif
    of the roman capitals as their model. Which, as you imply, is significant. But
    my basic point remains: the swelling of the stem in this construction is not indicative
    of an expansion stroke, as you suggested, but is the normal characteristic of
    this kind of serif as produced with a translation tool.\r\n\r\nThe distinction
    between written and 'built-up' letters tends to get blurred in the most formal
    styles of any script. The nature of formal, as distinct from cursive, writing
    involves the pen leaving the paper more often, and sometimes addition of non-fundamental
    strokes to complete a particular shape associated with the style. In some classical
    Arabic styles, for instance, certain shapes are augmented with the corner of the
    pen and then filled; this despite the fact that the overall appearance looks very
    fluid and Arabic writing is frequently referred to as 'cursive' on account of
    its connectivity. Writing is a very varied and complex phenomenon, and it is a
    mistake to associate it only with the characteristics of e.g. modern handwriting.\r\n\r\nPeter:
    <em>I think what John might be highlighting, Bill, is that not only does the pen
    provide the foundational contrast template, but it also can provide a partial
    grammer of the kinds of modifications or adjustments that might be introduced.</em>\r\n\r\nYes,
    thank you, that expresses it very well. "
  created: '2007-10-12 19:01:12'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Peter: <em>The direction of the change is not anti-chirographic, though structurally
    the norm violation is dramatic when seen against the translational contrast paradigm.</em>\r\n\r\nOnly
    if you are presuming that the translation stroke paradigm does not permit rotation
    <em>between</em> strokes. But this is clearly not a requirement of the stroke
    paradigm, as the Byzantine Greek cursive style demonstrates: it is a matter of
    application of the paradigm to particular ways of writing. So what is violated
    in the z is the norm of the humanist bookhand way of writing (although I wonder
    if a sufficiently broad and deep search might reveal a precursor to this apparently
    typographic innovation?), not the translation stroke paradigm. What the possibility
    of rotation between strokes implies, of course, is that there is no such thing
    as a translational <em>contrast paradigm,</em> only translational contrast conventions
    of particular styles. Now, any style of writing based around the use of a broad
    tool is likely to make full use of contrast, i.e. it is unlikely to involve rotation
    such that contrast is eliminated. But nowhere does the translation stroke model
    imply a consistent angle or, hence, a consistent contrast pattern. The stroke
    model is a description of what happens <em>within the stroke.</em> What happens
    between the strokes, or on top of the strokes, is all application.\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-12 19:15:59'
- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "<em>So it is only anti-chirographic in a very limited sense.</em>\r\n\r\nYou're
    saying that flouting the established principles of chirography is still chirographically-driven.\r\nIt's
    anti-chirographic, rather than non-chirographic.\r\nThe alternative would be driven
    by some other, non-chirographic principle.\r\nBut I suspect you would argue that
    non-chirography is also a reaction to chirography.\r\nIs there no escape? Is it
    all about chirography?!"
  created: '2007-10-12 19:44:08'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201CSo what is violated in the z is the norm of the humanist bookhand way
    of writing.\u201D \r\n\r\nYou're right. So I would alter my sentence to say: The
    <em>direction</em> of the change is not anti-chirographic, though <em>structurally</em>
    the norm violation is dramatic when seen against the humanist translation-based
    conventions.\r\n\r\nI could add: in type \u2014 at least for a time \u2014 the
    humanist convention as regards contrast-syling became paradigmatic.\r\n\r\nWhen
    rotation between strokes is allowed, the norm violation is still dramatic, because
    the rotation is quite extreme, but probably less traumatic. The source or inspiration
    is still perhaps, what happens in other obliques.\r\n\r\nIn the humanist west
    \u2014 if I remember from my Raffia Initials background research \u2014 rotation
    between strokes first became allowed in the formal humanist majuscule, partly
    as a device to harmonize the Roman majuscule and the Carolinian miniscule."
  created: '2007-10-12 20:32:56'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Nick, from the point of view of the writing hand that spawned it, every font
    is at one and the same time a paraphrase and an escape. That a secondary chirographic
    reference or a geometrical principle \u2014 like inversion or the perfect circle,
    the square and the equilateral triangle \u2014 can illuminate the escape doesn't
    matter. It just helps you get a handle on the nature of the change. What's important
    is the extent, pattern and force of the mutations.\r\n\r\nI guess I'm more inclined
    to ask where it's headed than to speculate about if it's fighting against \u2014
    except perhaps with the moderns where a fight against becomes explicit, and in
    some way self-defeating. I guess it's a question of what's a more fruitful way
    to think about what's going on.\r\n\r\nIn the old-style the flouting is always
    subtended by a more foundational embrace. But the escapes are cumulative. In the
    Roman de la Roi the escapes are modulated by the dictates of an superimposed grid.
    It's not always a chirographic principle that structures the mutations. Still
    the child is always father to the man.\r\n\r\nBill's right about other pressures."
  created: '2007-10-12 22:13:52'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: I should add to what I wrote above, that each paraphrase / escape can provoke
    or generate its own paraphrase / escape, so  we get paraphrase upon paraphrase
    and escape upon escape.
  created: '2007-10-12 23:24:32'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Nick: <em>Is there no escape? Is it all about chirography?!</em>\r\n\r\nI
    think Wim Crouwel's new alphabet definitely escapes the chirographic. But that's
    the kind of radical approach necessary to sever the connection.\r\n\r\nI think
    as long as one is considering letter construction at all in terms of strokes,
    then the chirographic isn't far away. And bear in mind what I wrote today in response
    to Bill: our concept of writing shouldn't be limited by experience of Anglo-American
    handwriting, i.e. chirography implies all textual marks directly made with the
    hand and a tool, and that includes styles in which strokes are built up, or outlined
    and filled, as found in various writing traditions around the world.\r\n\r\nIn
    order to get away from chirographic models or influences, you need to come up
    with a different conception of what the glyph is, i.e. something other than a
    collection of strokes. There are designers who have done this. I don't take Crouwel's
    approach very seriously, since the result doesn't actually work for text, but
    the idea of a glyph as a geometrically circumscribed space is, for better or for
    worse, a genuine break with chirography. It is different also from the attempts
    at geometric letter construction as proposed by D\xFCrer, Feliciano et al, because
    they were trying to discover a geometric basis for letters that were already being
    written, while Crouwel was using geometry to discover new letters.\r\n\r\nMore
    interesting, though, is the approach to the glyph as a figure-ground relationship,
    as seen in much of Frutiger's work and in Evert Bloemsma's types."

  created: '2007-10-13 16:51:02'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "John, thinking through letterforms in figure / ground (Frutiger / Modernistic[?])
    or form / counterform (Bloemsma in Legato / Postmodern[?]) terms can also illuminate
    \u2018escapes\u2019 from \u201Cspecific-contrast-type\u201D keyed paraphrase in
    the sense I suggested above.\r\n\r\nFurthermore, I think Wim Crouwel's New Alphabet
    still has the structure of paraphrase in important ways, as do Douglas Hofstadter
    and his gang's Letter Spirit alphabets. The paraphrase / escape dynamic here is
    not in terms of contrast styling but in terms of construction, or what I've called
    role architecture. \r\n\r\nEach paraphrase is an escape, but the only way to escape
    paraphrase is to switch to an unwritten script."

    is easy or natural to the pen, and what is build up with geometrical guidance,
    guidance by some notion of notan or whatever--because any shape can be drawn by
    building up. \r\n\r\nAlso, if I understand Noordzij's views rightly--and they
    are the topic of this thread--he would not count shapes carefully built up from
    multiple strokes as 'writing'. "
  created: '2007-10-14 20:58:43'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>Also, if I understand Noordzij\u2019s views rightly\u2014and they
    are the topic of this thread\u2014he would not count shapes carefully built up
    from multiple strokes as \u2019writing\u2019.</em>\r\n\r\nAs Peter notes, Noordzij
    shows exactly this phenomenon in the context of Burgundian miniscule, in which
    overalapping strokes are essential creating the letters. Of course this is writing,
    just as it is writing when an Arabic scribe deftly outlines and fills the top
    of the isolated or initial ayn in the context of creating naskh text: that is
    <em>how</em> that script is written. Are you saying that no one, ever in history,
    has 'written' Classical roman capitals because the way to write them involves
    going back over the stem to create the second serif? It seems to me that you have
    a very limited notion of what constitutes writing.\r\n\r\nBelow is Fr Catich's
    illustration of how to write the Trajan letters with a broad brush (sorry it is
    out of focus: it's too late for natural light here and I don't like the flash).
    The same technique is shown in any calligraphy manual treating the writing of
    the Roman capitals with a broad nib. These letters are one way of writing the
    Latin alphabet. The technique they use is non-cursive, i.e. the pen leaves the
    page during the construction of the letters, which is the crucial distinction
    between formal (formata) and cursive (corsiva) styles of writing. Whether the
    pen comes back down and goes over an existing stroke or comes down and makes an
    entirely separate stroke depends on the form of the individual letter and the
    conventions of the individual style.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/catichstrokes_6517.jpg]\r\n\r\n<em>If
    you allow built up shapes as \u2019strokes\u2019, then everything is allowed...</em>\r\n\r\nI
    didn't say I allowed built up shapes as strokes, I allow them as <em>writing.</em>
    I also consider cuneiform text made by pressing a stylus into soft clay writing:
    the direct result of hand and tool, with a distinctive pattern of shapes resulting
    from the movement of the hand and the nature of the tool. There is plenty of writing
    in the world that is not stroke based in its creation. In the case of the Roman
    capitals written with a broad brush or nib, though, we are definitely talking
    about stroke based writing, and the fact that bits of strokes are written on top
    of other strokes doesn't alter the fact, any more than it does for the Burgundian
    miniscule.\r\n\r\nNow, insofar as some methods of writing might be said to contravene
    a stroke method -- the treatment of the top of the ayn in naskh certainly does,
    and the outlined and filled mediaeval versals are a good example of a style that
    many people might have trouble classifying as 'writing' --, exactly the same questions
    may be asked about them as we have been asking about typographic letters, i.e.
    to what degree are they 'paraphrase' of the stroke and to what degree are they
    'escape'. Note: note paraphrase or escape writing, but paraphrase or escape the
    stroke. What this means, of course, is that in this aspect too of text creation
    typography doesn't really introduce anything wholly new under the sun."

  created: '2007-10-15 06:11:22'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "John, I am aware of the method of drawing capitals that you have illustrated.
    And indeed I referred to it above in answering you. \r\n\r\nBut also I wrote that
    that is <em>not</em> what is going on in the Garamond lower case letters I posted.
    The difference is subtle, but crucial, and indeed illustrates the main point that
    I have been arguing. \r\n\r\nCatich\u2019s illustration shows how to draw capitals
    with an extra stroke for one side of a fairly symmetrical serif. Catich I believe
    says that these were painted with a brush, and I think it is a stretch it to say
    that the style of monumental capitals on the Trajan column and other stone carving
    is a copy of handwriting. But that is not essential to my argument. \r\n\r\nWhat
    is essential is that your illustration shows exactly what I said is the difference
    between Garamond and this way of writing or painting a capital. The Catich illustration
    has a *straight* stem, with an added flair at the bottom.\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Garamond
    Gros Canon n_4217.gif] \r\n\r\nThe Garamond letters above do not have straight
    stems. As the attached \u2018n\u2019 from Garamond\u2019s Gros Cannon shows, the
    stems swell gradually beginning just below the join of the branch. This swelling
    is an <em>expansion</em> of the shape as it descends below the join. You could
    get such expansion by deliberate building but you will not get it simply by a
    stroke of the broad pen to add a serif at the bottom of the letter. Just to blend
    the serif into the stroke smoothly you do not need to go that high on the stem,
    and then sharply change the curvature of the stroke as you go down. And as you
    conceded, the humanist script lower case in any case was not written with such
    two stroke serifs at the bottom. \r\n\r\nThe difference between handwriting and
    built up letters is crucial here. I am pointing out that that Garamond is doing
    built-up lettering or, more precisely, sculpted letters--and these consciously
    and systematically depart from the stroke of the pen when writing letters by hand.
    \r\n\r\nJust to be clear about the difference, Noordzij writes very emphatically
    that built-up letter shapes not do not count as handwriting (p. 9 of  <em>The
    Stroke</em>):\r\n\r\n\u201CThe first, initial, fundamental shape is the single
    track of a tool. Only handwriting preserves the characteristics of the single
    stroke. <em>Handwriting</em> is single-stroke writing. <em>Lettering</em> is writing
    with built-up shapes. In lettering the shapes are more patient than in handwriting,
    as they accept retouching strokes that may gradually improve (or impair) the quality
    of the shapes. Lettering is independent of the tool, but this freedom is only
    available at the expense of character: in writing composed of overlapping strokes
    the shapes of single strokes get lost...\u201D\r\n\r\nI have been arguing all
    along that type deliberately and systematically departs from handwriting, even
    though it is strongly influenced by it. It departs to serve both aesthetics and
    readability. And there are traditions in which the departures of earlier type
    designers influence later type designers. \r\n\r\nHere in Garamond\u2019s lower
    case we have one such departure and tradition. I do not see this \u2018waisted\u2019
    stroke in the humanist writing you posted, or other humanist writing. I do see
    it in Garamond, and then in later type designs, such as those of Caslon, two centuries
    later. And that is because there are type traditions, traditions that contradict
    the natural stroke of the pen. Fred Smeijers, author of <em>Counter Punch</em>,
    Smeijers sees punch-cutting as an essential influence on typeface design. And
    not co-incidentally, he uses the punch-cutter\u2019s sculptured stem waist in
    some of his types. \r\n\r\nPunch-cutting is the sculpting of letters, not writing.
    And that difference, as Noordzij notes, gives a freedom that the pen lacks, for
    good or ill. The putting of \u2018waists\u2019 into lower case characters, I believe
    for reasons of even color as well as aesthetics, is one example of how punch-cutters
    took advantage of that freedom to create shapes that contradicted the written
    line of the broad pen of fixed width.  \r\n\r\nPunch-cutting, and not only writing,
    is an important influence on design. Here I think Harry Carter and Fred Smeijers
    have good points to make.  \r\n\r\nNow I recognize that there is some ambiguity
    here. At what point do overlapped strokes, which occur naturally in writing, become
    built-up shapes? I would submit that when you systematically alter a shape that
    was never drawn that way for lower case letters, and differently even what would
    be done for Caps, and in a way not called for by smooth shapes, then you are building
    letters, not writing. It is just Noordzij\u2019s deep analysis of handwriting
    that has helped me to see what is not handwriting, but sculpture. \r\n\r\nType
    wasn\u2019t written, it was punch cut, and that is a key fact that changed type
    design. It didn\u2019t remove the powerful influence of the pen, but it did enable
    other influences to be expressed and other goals to be served, in ways that were
    not only different, but sometimes even contradicted the stroke of the broad pen.
    \r\n"
  created: '2007-10-15 14:39:57'
- author:
    name: enne_son

  body: "\u201CIt is just my respect for Noordzij\u2019s deep analysis of hand writing
    that has helped me to see what is not hand writing, but sculpture.\u201D [Bill]\r\n\r\nI
    think this and the other things you write are right Bill. A Noordzijian analytic
    of type design helps you see what is paraphrase and what escape, and helps you
    gauge the direction of the escape, and through that how it is paradigmatic of
    the culture it reflects.\r\n\r\nYet, other analytics of type design exist and
    have value. And where Noordzij\u2019s stops they might take over. Or it can work
    the other way. I\u2019ve always liked the one implicit in Douglas Hofstadter's
    response to Donald Knuth's concept of a Metafont (Visible Language, Volume XVI
    #4 (Autumn 1982). Others might like others.\r\n\r\nNoordzij's illimuniates in
    ways that others perhaps can't. But in my discussions with Noordzij about Hofstadter\u2019s
    he seems to see it as antithetical to his.\r\n\r\n"

    have helped to make swelling part of the grammer of norm-violational options,
    but here it seems to have a purely optical-grammatical reason, having to do with
    the size of the foot serifs."

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Stefan, that's a higher resolution scan of the same page as above, from Harry
    Carter's <em>A View of Early Typography</em>. If you are interested in history
    of type, it's one of the best books, and it's in paperback, not too expensive.
    \r\n\r\nThanks, John. I am not sure how best to characterize the departures of
    type from handwriting. To investigate it properly would involve taking good scans
    of Jenson, Griffo, and Garamond, blowing them up and writing over them with a
    broad pen. That would be very interesting, but not something I'm likely to do
    any time soon.  \r\n\r\n>I\u2019ve always liked the [analysis of letter shapes]
    implicit in Douglas Hofstadter\u2019s response to Donald Knuth\u2019s concept
    of a Metafont (Visible Language, Volume XVI #4 (Autumn 1982). Others might like
    others. ...But in my discussions with Noordzij about Hofstadter\u2019s he seems
    to see it as antithetical to his.\r\n\r\nPeter, I think the failure of Knuth's
    Metafont as a way of creating letters is very interesting and perhaps revealing
    of the differences between handwriting and type. I believe Knuth's ideas appeared
    in print at about the same time as Noordzij's, and have some striking similarities,
    along with differences. Noordzij writes of a moving shape--tip of the pen--and
    its rotation and expansion. Knuth I believe has a single line, and rules of expansion
    of that line. \r\n\r\nCan you briefly summarize Hofstedter's view? Does Noordzij
    object to Knuth, or Hofstedter, or both?\r\n\r\nJohn has, as I remember, explained
    that in developing non-latin fonts he sometimes uses the pen to understand the
    ductus of a non-latin script. But then for creating a font he \"sculpts\" digital
    outlines, like almost everybody else these days. \r\n\r\nThis process of understanding
    the underlying writing, but actually sculpting outlines, I think reflects the
    reality of type--that it is modified handwriting. Both the fact that it has a
    base of handwriting and the modifications are essential. But the much of the work
    of type design is in sculpting the outlines. \r\n\r\nThe fact is that we could
    use digital pens to produce fonts, but in general we don't. And the more pen-influenced
    approach of Metafont resulted in poor-looking fonts, according to Richard Southall
    in his book 'Printers Types in the 20th Century'. \r\n\r\nSo I'm thinking that
    the departures from writing, both for readability and aesthetics, may be a quite
    large percentage of what is involved in type design. \r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-16 18:36:32'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201Cthe departures from writing, both for readability and aesthetics\u201D
    [Bill]\r\n\r\nHow can I explain to you why I object to this way of putting things?
    \r\n\r\nWriting is neither unreadable, nor is it ugly. The departures are not
    \u2018for\u2019 readability and aesthetics in the sense that there are or were
    readability and aesthetic problems with writing in its benchmark instantiations
    or as a category, that type as a category tries in a progressively ascending way
    to solve or address. \r\n\r\nThe problems that departures from the specifics of
    specific writing conventions try to solve or address are, as I tried to say, problems
    generated by what happens optical-grammatically when a specific convention of
    writing is paraphrased in sculpted forms on the ends of recangular blocks of metal,
    which force them a certain distance apart and to rigorously conform to a carefully
    structure height / width grid. Paraphrase is what sculpting has to be or do, if
    it is to continue using the communcation convention that was sparked and shaped
    by direct mark-making.\r\n\r\nDepartures from writing are not essential, but feature
    manipulation in the context of paraphrastic stratagies is unavoidable, characteristic
    of type design, and can be productive.\r\n\r\nAbout the Douglas Hofstadter response
    to Donald Knuth and how what Donald Knuth proposed relates to Noordzij I\u2019ll
    transcribe a section from Hofstader\u2019s Abstract, and point you to the Epilogue
    of Noordzij's RIDT paper as a start. The first sentence of Hofstadter's extract
    reads: \u201CIt is argued that readers are likely to carry away from Donald Knuth's
    article \u201CThe concept of a Meta-Font\u201D a falsely optimisitc view of the
    extent to which the design of typefaces and letterforms can be mechanized through
    an approach depending on describing letterforms by specifying the settings of
    a large number of parameters.\u201D\r\n\r\nIn this context Hofstadter outlines
    a way to letterform descritption using \u2018quirk-notes\u2019 to define how in
    a specific font, \u2018roles\u2019 across the alphabet are filled. For example
    the \u2018stem\u2019 role or the \u2018bowl\u2019 role, or the \u2018ascender\u2019
    role, and how the roles relate, that is, the styles of their joining."
  created: '2007-10-16 19:48:59'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, to reply to another thread in your comment: The poor quality of Metafont
    font\u2019s isn't really a product of the fact that it was pen-influenced, but
    that a mechanically tractable parametrization of a mechanical pen\u2019s enabled
    behaviours and dimensions can't respond effectively to the optical-grammatical
    issues that adjusting values over those parameters evokes."
  created: '2007-10-16 21:49:09'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">Writing is neither unreadable, nor is it ugly.\r\n\r\nWriting can be beautiful
    or ugly, just like type. Perhaps I should have said for 'stylistic reasons'. The
    point is that you might want to modify a glyph in a way that is further from writing
    to get the look you want. To take a clear-cut example, the Futura lower case 'a'
    was an attempt to be geometrical and have no trace of the movement of the pen
    in it. \r\n\r\nAs to readability, I accept the old notion that extended text is
    most readable when set in a lower case serifed roman, and every departure from
    that decreases ease of reading, and possibly speed. Certainly I think hand writing
    can be quite readable, but as a rule in extended text it is much less easy to
    read than printing. The formal hands were partly an attempt at regularity that
    helps reading, but I don't think even the best of the formal hands is as easy
    to read as good type, well set. \r\n\r\n>The poor quality of Metafont font\u2019s
    isn\u2019t really that a product of the fact that it was pen-influenced, but that
    a mechanically tractable parametrization of a mechanical pen\u2019s enabled behaviours
    and dimensions can\u2019t respond effectively to the optical-grammatical issues
    adjusting values over those parameters evokes.\r\n\r\nI think you mean \"mathematically\"
    rather than \"mechanically\", as what is involved here are equations, not a physical
    device.\r\n\r\nIn any case the problem is not, I think, finding the mathematics
    to express each desirable modification. It is that you'd have to have modifications
    for each letter of each font, so it is quicker just to design the damn outline.
    \r\n\r\nTo put it another way the modifications are just do specific, and the
    what pleases the eye too complex and unknown as yet to produce a general mathematical
    model what would cover all cases, give a relatively small set of 'quirk-notes',
    as Hofstedter calls them.\r\n\r\nFor example, the stem on the B is often reduced
    by a few units relative to the H, which helps even color, as the bowls of the
    B will otherwise make the stem look a little fat. And so sometimes is the last,
    thick stem of the M reduced. Again, you have some visual crowding with all those
    diagonals in a small space, and lightening the thing helps. Question: What is
    the rule for how much you should reduce the M, given the B? Answer: there isn't
    any rule. For the eye, it will probably depend on the contrast, boldness, serif
    if any, width of the glyph, optical size, etc. etc. \r\n\r\nAnd different people
    will make different decisions. Matthew Carter will make good decisions. Some others
    won't. Carter's font will read well, and look good, and another's might not. Still
    others will make good decisions different from Carter's. Type design, as has been
    often noted by such experienced designers as G. Briem, is a series of thousands
    of decisions--at least one or two for each glyph, in each style, and sometimes
    in each weight.\r\n\r\nNow the thing about those decisions, such as on the stems
    of the B and the M, is that they are <em>type design</em> decisions, not <em>writing</em>
    decisions. They concern details that are at a level of precision that is generally
    not possible in writing, but is possible, and indeed demanded by a professional
    quality text font. \r\n\r\nIt is because I am right now wrestling every day with
    these demands of a quality text font that I am so insistent that very many of
    these decisions are not writing decisions, and that they do make an important
    difference. Perhaps Noordzij was unimpressed by the 'quirk notes' idea for similar
    reasons. They aren't quirks, they are type design.  \r\n\r\nOf course you could
    say with some justice that all type designers are quirky, but that's what it takes
    :) "

  body: "Bill: <em>This process of understanding the underlying writing, but actually
    sculpting outlines, I think reflects the reality of type\u2014that it is modified
    handwriting. Both the fact that it has a base of handwriting and the modifications
    are essential. But the much of the work of type design is in sculpting the outlines.</em>\r\n\r\nYes,
    I agree with that. I also have been thinking, today, about how this discussion
    relates to practice and, particularly, to my own practice. I'm trying to account
    for the fact that the typefaces I make are full of the kind of modifications to
    which you refer -- that is, they are evident in what I create -- and at the same
    time they don't seem to me very significant in terms of this discussion. And I
    think what it comes down to is that, for the most part, these modifications are
    something that I apply in the manufacturing stage to make individual glyphs 'work',
    rather than something central to my thinking about letterforms."

- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201Cthat's why I'm so insistent that very many of these decisions are not
    writing decisions, and that they do make an important difference\u201D \r\n\r\nBill,
    do you take me to say that they <em>are</em> writing decisions, and that they
    don't amount to a hill of beans? \r\n\r\nI tried to specify in non-generic ways
    in what sense I think they are essential by bringing up issues of optical-grammatical
    imperatives and gestural atmospheric wants. And I tried to point to in what sense
    they are important by bringing up matters of the consolidation and extendion of
    affordances, as a kind of epiphenomenal benefit or gift. Is there more that must
    be said that this doesn't say?\r\n\r\nI don't want to give a chance to readers
    taking from that fact (or statements like the one I targetted), that the changes
    arose \u2014 or came about \u2014 <em>because of</em> perceived or intrinsic difficulties
    reading benchmark writing, or dissatisfactions with how writing looked. That just
    seems to me to be a damaging, derogatory and unhistorical way of conceptualizing
    the changes that took place.\r\n\r\nI've also asked before how much of \u2019ease\u2019
    is conditioning, and how much affordance. So that to has to be factored in.\r\n\r\nAnd
    I've tried to say that while the results of genuinely optical-grammatically motivated
    adjustments sometimes have precedants in writing, this isn't ever or always the
    whole story. These optical-grammatically motivated adjustments might in other
    instances reflect other disciplines, like deliberate figure / ground gauging or
    form counterform experimentation as in the cases of Frutiger and Bloemsma's Legato.\r\n\r\nThe
    computer <em>is</em> a mechanical device, but it's parametrization is algorized,
    which is where the math comes in. Parameterized design can't make optical-grammatical
    adjustments, because it can't predict where they might be needed; but these are
    the essence of what it takes to make glyphs \u2018work\u2019 together and well
    in the conditions of use they're destined for. So that's where the blood sweat
    and tears in type will always lie.\r\n\r\nAnd finally, microtypographical adjustments
    are not inconsequential quirks, but if they are functional, deliberate, historical
    and systematic, they can be described in \u2018quirk-notes\u2019 because they
    are particulare to the individual glyph-set or style."

  body: "Bill, let me state my discomfort in a different way.\r\n\r\nWhen you say
    you don't think even the best or the formal hands is as easy to read as good type,
    I want to ask: Is this fair? In what sense is this true? Is part of this your
    conditioning? What part is real affordances? Is the plateau of good affordance
    really that narrow? What does it mean to say this? What is the importance of,
    relevance of, or motivation for saying this? \r\n\r\nAnd to relate this to your
    more general statement: What does the acceptence of your \u2018old notion\u2019
    represent? What part of comfort is familiarity with a particular role-architectural
    conceit? What part of affordance has to do with optical-grammatical noise? How
    do I distinguish between optical-grammatical noise and gestural atmospheric tuning?
    Is the one the method for \u2014 but anti-thetical to \u2014 the other? What part
    does gestural-atmospheric vitality play in broad experiential judgements of readability?
    Are seriffed types intrinsically more readable or just less susceptable to visual
    wordform resolutional decay when tracking for justification is introduced? How
    sould all that impact what we do in specific contexts of use?\r\n\r\nAre we not
    back with rule versus law?\r\n\r\nOr is it that I like to complicate things?"
