- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">optical-grammatical imperatives and gestural atmospheric wants ...are important
    by bringing up matters of the consolidation and extension of affordances, as a
    kind of epiphenomenal benefit or gift. Is there more that must be said that this
    doesn\u2019t say?\r\n\r\nWell I may misunderstand you, as without concrete examples
    to illustrate your statements I find it hard to be sure what you mean. \r\n\r\nI
    take it by 'consolidation and extension of affordances' you mean that the type
    becomes more readable. I don't think this is a by-product or \"gift\" of the sense
    that type designers have of something being 'not right' and feeling a need to
    adjust proportions of a glyph or weights of parts to make it 'work', as John put
    it. \r\n\r\nMy feeling is that a big part of the sense of something not being
    right relates to good rhythm and even color in the type. These are the <em>same
    thing</em> as what promotes readability, and indeed makes good text types more
    readable than handwriting. So a large part of 'working' and 'being right' is readability--not
    a different variable of which has the consequence of helping readability. \r\n\r\nSo
    if you mean by 'epiphenomenon' a by-product, then I don't agree. But if you mean
    that the sense of rightness includes good color and rhythm, which are part of
    readability, then I agree.\r\n\r\n>[Changes came about] because of perceived or
    intrinsic difficulties reading benchmark writing, or dissatisfactions with how
    writing looked. That just seems to me to be a damaging, derogatory and unhistorical
    way of conceptualizing the changes that took place.\r\n\r\nI did not mean to imply
    that type designers were dissatisfied with the way writing looked. As I said above,
    I liked your analysis that the regularity of type gave rise to a new standard.
    Variations that would have been submerged in the general variability of handwriting
    became obtrusive because of repetition of the same characters over and over again
    in type. But the net effect of being hit with what looks 'wrong' and the new ability
    in punch cutting to control outlines to a finer degree of tolerance had the result--through
    the eyes and hands and imagination of great designers--of type that surpassed
    the readability of handwriting. "
  created: '2007-10-17 13:49:28'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "\u201Cnot a different variable\u201D\r\n\r\nBill, \u2018readability\u2019
    is not \u2018a\u2019 variable in any well-specified and scientifically tractable
    or historiographically and pedagogically productive sense, and judgements of readability,
    though important, are anecdotal experiential reports that involve a cluster of
    partially isolatible, but interrelated factors, which we might make ourselves
    aware of. Some have to do with realities of structuration on the object side,
    and some have to do with realities of conditioning and bias on the subject side.
    I\u2019d love if you could affirm this, but than I\u2019d want to ask why you
    won\u2019t let this temper your broad generalizations in ways that I and maybe
    others might find them easier to embrace.\r\n\r\nIn my book rhythm and even colour,
    as well as form / counterform logic and figure / ground control are optical-grammatical
    issues that have a lot to do with structuration on the object side. And the affordances
    that I am interested in are perceptual discrimination offordances and visual wordform
    resolutional affordances. Solving optical grammatical issues in one way might
    be productive or counterproductive when it comes to consolidating affordances.
    A type might become too regular. But these things aren't so easily decidable just
    by direct optical-grammatical gauging. I think all type-involved people should
    push beyond simple, conditioned statements to that level of awareness.  \r\n\r\nPerceptual
    discrimination has to do with how well the eye can make out the criterial parts
    of letters in the context of the word, sentence and text-block wholes these parts
    are part of; wordform resolution has to do with how easily and resiliently these
    parts in their relation form letters that gel well into stable, unitary, robust
    word-objects, because of the way they are open to and relate to other letters.\r\n\r\nI
    mean by consolidation and extension of affordances, that the types became more
    resistant to visual wordform resolutional collapse in tougher and a wider range
    of contexts of use. This comes out as readable in smaller sizes, or readable under
    greater print degredation. But I don't want to draw from these specifics that
    type is intrinsically more readable than writing, or one type than the other,
    on some absolute readability measure. All I think it is warranted to say is that
    affordance structures differ from type to type: some are deep and broad, others
    are pointed, rich but narrow. I would be happier to talk about readability or
    affordance profiles, using my kind of distinctions, than I am about readability
    as such in a kind of winner take all scheme.\r\n\r\nGiven that the experience
    of comfort in reading is distinctly and complexly multifactorial in the ways I
    try to describe, to make broad \u2018ranking\u2019 statements using \u2018readability\u2019
    as a measure is a strategy whose productive, pedagogic, and \u2018fairness\u2019
    value I don't fully see.\r\n\r\nBut maybe I've banged this gong enough for now,
    and should move on to other things."

- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "Peter, I think I cross-posted with your previous post. Your most recent post
    I have great difficulty in understanding, so let me respond primarily to the previous
    one. \r\n\r\nI gather that you reject my claim that good type, well set, is more
    readable for lengthy text than is the best handwriting. \r\n\r\nYes, this is a
    generalization, and I may well be wrong. I thought you approved of Noordzij's
    tentative 'laws', rather than authoritarian rules. My proposal is a tentative
    or conjectural 'law' if you will, not an authoritarian demand that I am imposing
    on anybody. \r\n\r\nAlso I'm not accusing anybody of incompetence, bad intentions,
    or bad actions, so I don't understand what 'fair' or 'unfair' have do with it.
    I am right or mistaken, that's all. \r\n\r\nAlso, just to be clear, I am not saying
    that all decisions that historical creators of type made were based on readability.
    What I am saying is that some of them have been, and those concerning even color
    and good rhythm resulted in type being more readable than handwriting.\r\n\r\nAnd
    that is not because type creators they were any better workmen or artists than
    the scribes. It is because the medium offered greater control over outlines, and
    in a more limited way, spacing. And some superb type creators, such as Garamond,
    were able to take advantage of this greater control. \r\n\r\nReadability, or ease
    of reading with good comprehension is not a scientifically well defined and proven
    concept. But I'm not writing a scientific paper, just expressing my opinion in
    language that has been used in typography for a long time. I cannot prove that
    my impression of greater readability is not simply due to habituation and familiarity,
    and rather than an inherent advantage of type. However I think there is reason
    to believe that regularity in rhythm and even color do ease reading. And the fact
    is that handwriting is almost never used for extended text, even though technically
    it could be written, photographed and printed in just as many copies as any typeset
    text. "

- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, other pressures are making it important that I back off.\r\n\r\nYou
    say you gather that I reject your claim that good type, well set, is more readable
    for lengthy text than is the best handwriting. \r\n\r\nI said I have number of
    issues with the making of this kind of statement, and would rather make a statement
    of a somewhat different kind, than be forced to choose for or against such a claim.
    To say that good type well set is more readable than the best handwriting is in
    fact derogatory, because it diminishes just how well the best writing does afford
    sustained reading of lengthy texts for those for whom it was the norm. And derogation
    is not fair.\r\n\r\nI would rather try and describe affordance ranges and thresholds
    and plateaus for different types in different conditions of use, than make simple
    binary across the board judgements of more or less. \r\n\r\nI'm expressing a measure
    of discomfort with the adequacy of the language that has been used in typography
    for a long time. So I'm asking myself, in a kind of Kantian way, what kind of
    a statement is a judgement of readability and legibility? And I try and punder
    perceptual psychology and other design traditions to find some tighter terms.
    The risk is  distinctions that are somehow more demanding, but also maybe more
    servicable. \r\n\r\nThe more important thing is to communicate my discomfort.
    Maybe that's just me making a mountain of a mole-hill. Maybe it's not widely shared.
    I don't know. And I'm not sure how I can take this further. I'll have to let it
    sit.\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-17 20:12:38'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "I'm having a little trouble letting this thing sit just yet, so I'll give
    it another go.\r\n\r\nAfter some more reflection, here is what I see now through
    my \u2018perceptual processing in reading\u2019 lens. (It probably brings me closer
    to what you, Bill, feel to be the case, but this way of framing it eases my reluctance.)\r\n\r\nI'll
    start with this: Gerrit Noordzij sees the middle ages as the period of the consolidation
    of the word.\r\n\r\nIn writing with the broad-nibbed pen the consolidation of
    affordances approached a kind of stall-point or highpoint or limit around the
    time type made its entrance. In type the consolidation dynamic could continue
    apace concurrently or be productively resumed because of the less constrained
    feature manipulation it allowed. Feature manipulation overtly addresses optical-grammatical
    (\u2018look\u2019) issues (like regularity, or evenness of colour) and gestural-atmospheric
    (\u2018feel\u2019) wants (like, greater angularity or cursivity or varied stress),
    all the while \u2014 just under the surface of conscious awareness \u2014 re-mapping
    visual-wordform resolutional affordances, quietly adjusting its thresholds and
    ranges, and basis.\r\n\r\nBecause it re-tunes the basis, it requires a small-incremental
    reconditioning of it\u2019s victims (a pick-up phrase originating in some other
    context from some of my conversations with Noordzij). But the re-tuning of the
    thresholds and ranges are real readability gains. \r\n\r\nSo to me this says the
    greater feature-manipulational flexibility in punchcutting, could initiate a consolidation
    of the readability affordances of writing beyond writing, though not without a
    cost. The cost is growing distance from the readability and character of first-rate
    scribal writing, especially when you factor in the unfamiliarity of the foreign
    or earlier mother tongue.\r\n"

  body: "William: \"Also, just to be clear, I am not saying that all decisions that
    historical creators of type made were based on readability. What I am saying is
    that some of them have been, and those concerning even color and good rhythm resulted
    in type being more readable than handwriting.\r\n\r\nAnd that is not because type
    creators they were any better workmen or artists than the scribes. It is because
    the medium offered greater control over outlines, and in a more limited way, spacing.
    And some superb type creators, such as Garamond, were able to take advantage of
    this greater control.\"\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nAs we all know, control over outlines does
    not always result in superior outlines. Look at the state of type design today.\r\n\r\nMany
    think that type well set is of minor importance compared to type design in the
    determination of readability. I care to differ. I could take Garamond and make
    it very difficult to read even at 12 point printed crisply. No, I do not mean
    by removing anyone's reading glasses.\r\n\r\nAlso, some pain from \"unreadable
    type faces\", such as Bodoni, can be eased by careful setting.\r\n\r\nQuickly
    handwriting became more of a \"hangover and not so much a model for type design\".\r\n\r\nTools
    do affect outlines, not to mention right handed people have dominated left handed.
    Counter punches compared to gravures, the two schools of thought? Now the cold
    mechanical feeling of digital type. This could be a lengthy discussion but I am
    going to spare us all.\r\n\r\nThe church found Gutenberg's Bible of minor importance
    compared to the lavish financial profits from the indulgences he printed. The
    business of printing dictated many different objectives. As I mentioned much earlier,
    Aldus Manutius invented the pocket book. There was great interest in making types
    readable in smaller sizes. This saved not only paper but allowed for more pages
    in the chase thus reducing press time.\r\n\r\nIs handwriting more readable/legible,
    or even possible at six point than type? I think not! I predict, if it is possible
    to handwrite that small, (avoiding an embarrassing surprise) it must be hard to
    read at all. Besides I don't think they made a goose feather small enough. For
    the record, I believe the smallest hand set type ever made was 2 1/2 point, probably
    commissioned by an unscrupulous lawyer.\r\n\r\nYou may have noticed in an eye
    examination letters are used. Well, they aren't in Finland. Still, keep in mind
    the conspiracy between French eye glass manufactures and printers. They both had
    much to gain by smaller type sizes. Not to mention, the consumer was advantaged
    financially. Well, they all were.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n "

  body: "Peter: <em>So to me this says the greater feature-manipulational flexibility
    in punchcutting, could initiate a consolidation of the readability affordances
    of writing beyond writing...</em>\r\n\r\nThis is better stated and doubtless more
    accurate than my attempt to get at this idea by looking at feature manipulation
    as an extension of the stroke logic inherited from writing. I like this idea of
    consolidation of a process that is already taking place in writing. It fits well
    with examples from a number of different scripts, in which one sees formal styles
    of writing develop that begin, sometimes in individual features or letters, to
    break with 'the single track of a tool'. [It is because of such instances that
    I don't fully agree with a definition of writing that completely rules out occasional
    stroke repetition, build up or outline.]\r\n\r\n...<em>though not without a cost.
    The cost is growing distance from the readability and character of first-rate
    scribal writing, especially when you factor in the unfamiliarity of the foreign
    or earlier mother tongue.</em>\r\n\r\nThis is an observable cost, and it also
    has parallels within typography as in e.g. the loss of ability of many people
    to read fraktur with any ease or comfort. I wrote earlier in this discussion that
    we could not say whether e.g. Griffo's types were significantly more readable
    than the scribal book hand <em>for people who were used to reading that hand,</em>
    i.e. the contemporary audience for both kinds of books. Type could make consistently
    readable text at smaller sizes, and hence had an economical advantage, but at
    equivalent sizes I'm not convinced that there was any significant gain in readability.
    The 'consolidation of the readability affordances' were expressed in a reduction
    in the size of text. I wonder if any records survive indicating a rise in the
    number of lense grinders working in Venice at the same time?\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-19 00:52:01'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "First of all, just to be clear, I am not disparaging the accomplishments
    of scribes. There were over 3000 years of scribes writing alphabetic scripts and
    now something over 500 years of type. The scribes already did a great deal to
    make writing readable, including not only letter proportions, but also key matters
    of layout, such as amount of letter and word spacing, line measure, space between
    lines, and margins. And these, as Giampa points out, are also keys to readability.
    The scribes had already worked out much of the rules of readable text that we
    still follow.  \r\n\r\nHowever, there is a remaining area of difference of opinion
    I have with John and Peter, unless I have won Peter over in a discussion off-line.
    I think that evenness of spacing and color provides a relatively neutral framework
    or grid, within which the differences between letters are easily detected by the
    eye. When color or spacing is uneven, or there are other extraneous variations,
    this constitutes 'noise' that interferes with the detection of the 'signal' of
    letter differences. \r\n\r\nThat is why I still think that good type, well set,
    is more readable than good writing. Good writing still has more inherent 'noise'
    in it than good type, because of greater variability. The dimension of readability
    I have in mind here is how cognitively taxing it is to read text. This aspect
    of readability has not yet been tested, to my knowledge, but I have several times
    advocated such testing here on Typophile. I think that when good writing in familiar
    styles easily decoded by us--eg not Fraktur for most of us--is read it will prove
    more taxing than good printed text. And the more florid writing, with extraneous
    swashes etc, the more taxing it will be. \r\n\r\nIf this is a general psychological
    principle, we can reasonable apply it to readers in the 16th century, who didn't
    have greatly different DNA than we do. \r\n\r\nThis is also not to say that writing
    is less beautiful. In the hands of a superb calligrapher, it can be more beautiful--though
    less serviceable for long passages of text. "
  created: '2007-10-19 01:52:03'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, this probably needs a longer reply. But for now, think of noise first
    in terms of 'computation costs' at the visual cortex level. Also think of \u2018noise\u2019
    as productive in gestural-atmospheric terms. Noise is the basis of inflection,
    and inflection provides 'character' and character has appeal \u2014 another factor
    operating in our everyday experiential anecdotal judgements of readability. I
    still think your ranking judgement is too easily made. Type may be made to be
    more noise free, but is that equivalent to more readable?"
  created: '2007-10-19 04:44:30'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">Type may be made to be more noise free, but is that equivalent to more readable?\r\n\r\nI
    think there are a number of other factors involved, but that being able to get
    rid of noise is where type has the edge over handwriting. In other areas probably
    the best scribes were probably just as good for readability. \r\n\r\nOh on the
    'quirk notes' idea of Hofstedter. Where we do have enough information to automate
    things is when you have two weights of a design that resemble each other in the
    designer's eyes, then computer interpolation  create other weights that are very
    'family' looking. So as of now it seems to take two fonts with similar characters
    with a different values of a parameter to actually have the needed information
    to carry through the 'quirk notes' idea.. "
  created: '2007-10-19 05:03:10'
- author:
    name: enne_son
    picture: 109487
  body: "Bill, if reading affordance is a plateau with thresholds and ranges and heights,
    I might even become compelled to concede that not only ranges and thresholds but
    also heights are susceptible to adjustment beyond writing.\r\n\r\nBut I'll only
    accept ranking comparisons if they acknowledge: 1) that over time, type has probably
    re-tuned our internal maps so that we've become separated from the readability
    of writing to a degree; 2) (if they acknowledge) that through the broad-nibbed
    pen applied to a carolinian separated script, western writing had, by the time
    type arrived, carved out for its script a criterion-level or benchmark-level affordances
    plateau; 3) (if they acknowledge) that readability, or affordance in reading,
    is not a simple equation but a densely multifacted, delicately balanced thing.
    \r\n\r\nThe delicate balance thing is shown by the fact that removing noise in
    the optical-grammatical part of the equation might depress character in the gestural-atmospheric
    part of the equation. It might even be the case that some noise is necessary for
    the eyes to become and stay locked-on in visual terms to text, or for visual wordform
    resolution to stay robust, even though noise has a computational cost. Beyond
    a certain point, transparency might be a counter-productive chimera."
  created: '2007-10-19 13:13:36'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>I think there are a number of other factors involved, but that
    being able to get rid of noise is where type has the edge over handwriting. In
    other areas probably the best scribes were probably just as good for readability.</em>\r\n\r\nAgain,
    I think we have to take text size into account here. Where type has the most obvious
    edge over handwriting is in being able to produce smaller text with the same regularity
    and evenness of colour and spacing as a good scribe could produce at typically
    larger sizes.* This is a much more obvious advantage of type than the notion of
    type getting rid of noise, which I find very problematic, especially if we're
    comparing the textual culture of the first 100 years of printing from moveable
    metal type in Europe. The fact is that the vast majority of early printing was
    not very good: a lot of type was either over-inked or under-inked, the impressions
    were uneven (even on the same page), the metal alloys were not well developed
    and the type wore quickly, there is often a lot of extra-textual noise from ink
    spots. Even some of the most well-respected names in typographic history produced
    a lot of poorly printed books -- I know, I own some of them --, not just the justly
    admired masterpieces that get reproduced in books on type history. By comparison,
    the scribes -- seeking to compete in quality where they could not compete in quantity
    or economy -- were doing their best work during the same period, using highly
    prepared parchments and papers, well-maintained reeds and quills, carefully prepared
    inks. And if by 'noise' we include not only variation in letterforms or spacing
    but all those other factors that diminished the quality of so much printing then
    the manuscripts are by far the cleaner product.\r\n\r\n[The preparation of the
    writing surface in the Islamic scribal tradition is particularly important, because
    the reed pen is pushed so often when writing Arabic, and the sized papers they
    use are generally smoother than anything produced for either manuscript or print
    in Europe. The cleanest, sharpest letters I have ever seen were handwritten Arabic
    script.]\r\n\r\n\r\n* There are traditions of very small writing -- micrography
    -- in some scripts, notably Arabic and Hebrew. These are most often employed in
    decorative ways: you see a picture or a pattern, look at it more closely and you
    see that it contains a text in tiny letters. The Arabic term for such writing
    is <em>ghubar</em> -- literally 'dust' --, and a special style of writing was
    developed for it. The typical alif height of ghubar is less than 2mm; sometimes
    as small as 1.3mm. Because ghubar was sometimes used to write Qur'anic passages,
    legibility was, of course, extremely important."
  created: '2007-10-19 19:49:36'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: "John, you have a good point about the messiness of early printing. In looking
    at some 18th century printing in Caslon I was struck by how much of a mess it
    is, and painful to read by comparison to printing post-Baskerville, when inks
    and press techniques were improved. Unfortunately, the 'modern' high contrast
    types that became popular had their own problems with readability, in my view.
    \r\n\r\nThe benefits of the finer control that the designers introduced was only
    fully realized later. Still, the breakthroughs were made then. \r\n\r\nPeter,
    I don't think even spacing and color forces a designer to iron out variation in
    shapes. Indeed variation in shape is often needed to achieve even color, because
    of the very different shapes of different letters. For example, the tapering of
    diagonals prevents clotted joins, contributing to even color, but it also creates
    more lively, varied shapes in the alphabet. Accomplishing both liveliness and
    relatively little 'noise' is what makes great text faces great.   "
  created: '2007-10-19 20:09:18'
- author:
    name: Nick Shinn
    picture: 110193
  body: "<em>if by \u2019noise\u2019 we include not only variation in letterforms
    or spacing but all those other factors that diminished the quality of so much
    printing then the manuscripts are by far the cleaner product.</em>\r\n\r\nTrue,
    a good manuscript page is then cleaner than a bad printed page. But that kind
    of cleanliness isn't really relevant to the superiority of the typographic effect
    as described by Bill. If the benefit of type is to present more text on a page,
    within the grasp of an eye's scan, and less \"noise\", i.e. irregularity, then
    type has the edge, for two reasons.\r\n\r\nFirstly, type forms have shorter extenders
    and are more compactly line-spaced.\r\nSecondly, scribes introduce much irregularity
    with contextual forms and flourishes (even modest ones).\r\n\r\nOf course, scribes
    CAN produce stilted work which is as mechanically uniform and succint as type,
    but that is not their professional raison d'\xEAtre vis-a-vis the printing trade.
    Their added value is in handmade grace, which goes against typographic readability-value
    as defined by Bill in mechanical terms of maximizing quantity and uniformity of
    text.\r\n\r\nHowever, while accepting Bill's explanation for the superior readability
    of type <em>in one respect,</em> I don't believe that readability is quite so
    one-dimensional. It's not something that can be reduced to scientific or mechanistic
    proportions. That's fast food.\r\n"
  created: '2007-10-19 20:31:25'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">I don\u2019t believe that readability is quite so one-dimensional.\r\n\r\nI
    don't either, as I tried to make clear above. I was just referring to where type
    has excelled. There are a lot of things that contribute to easy readability of
    a typeface. Also the merits of a highly readable face can be totally subverted
    by bad setting. \r\n\r\nAn example of another factor in readability is darkness
    of the face. In the testing of Clearview as I remember they found that a medium
    weight sans was better than a bold for legibility in signs. I think there is also
    a sweet spot (or range) for text as far as darkness. But darkness is complicated,
    as perceived darkness is not simply a matter of sheer density. Reabability is
    quite complicated, I think, and not that well understood. I am not talking here
    about the obvious, like bad handwriting that you can't decipher to save your life,
    but rather readability at the high end--readable vs highly readable. "

    in the writing and typography of other scripts."
  created: '2007-10-20 05:43:36'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">writing and typography of other scripts.\r\n\r\nEver eager to test my hunches,
    I was looking over the Rabbi's shoulder this morning as he was chanting the Torah
    portion today. I've got to admit that the script--a now rare instance of handwriting
    of extended text by a professional scribe--was admirably lucid and readable, as
    well as quite beautiful. \r\n\r\nMy feeling is that the problems of getting even
    color in Hebrew script are a lot less, because of it being a square script. The
    problem in latin script is the presence of diagonal and round strokes, and their
    connections to stems. And the problem is bigger in lower case humanist script
    than caps.  When it is done well it is marvelously clear, as you have greater
    differentiation between letter shapes than in Hebrew or even roman caps. \r\n\r\nI
    really wonder how long the humanist script--with the lower case mimicking Roman
    capitals--would have lasted had not printing come along. Did the Carolingian miniscule
    last very long the first time around? I have the feeling that it is quite difficult
    to write well and quickly. And my impression is that even in formal documents,
    such as contracts, writing quickly became more cursive after the introduction
    of printing. Is this right?  "
  created: '2007-10-20 21:40:20'
- author:
    name: John Hudson
    picture: 110397
  body: "Bill: <em>I really wonder how long the humanist script\u2014with the lower
    case mimicking Roman capitals\u2014would have lasted had not printing come along.</em>\r\n\r\nA
    good question. On the one hand, one can guess that a major change in script style
    might not have occured until it did in fact occur with the influence of a new
    writing tool, but prior to the invention of type the scripts of Europe were constantly
    evolving and subject to alterations in compression, proportion, etc., and of course
    there were important regional scripts that gradually disappeared with the standardisation
    of roman types. Noordzij turns Bill's question on its head, wondering what might
    have happened if type had not frozen formal writing into those particular forms:
    \"A stop of 500 years in the development of text scripts is long enough to make
    one look upon formal writing as static. Against a continuous tradition of 5000
    years, however, the interruption has been merely incidental.'\r\n\r\n<em>And my
    impression is that even in formal documents, such as contracts, writing quickly
    became more cursive after the introduction of printing. Is this right?</em>\r\n\r\nIn
    Italy, chancery writing tended to be cursive anyway, but also highly elaborated
    with swashes and ornamental devices: so cursive in form, but without the characteristic
    speed of writing we associate with that term. The humanist roman was specifically
    a book hand, intended for literary, theological and scientific texts. The quickly
    written cursive, without elaboration, which is the mother of italic type, was
    a kind of note hand, often found in manuscripts copied for their content by individual
    scholars rather than by professional scribes (such manuscripts seem to me the
    very model for Aldus' small format books in italic type).\r\n\r\nIn England and
    some other parts of northern Europe, blackletter remained the norm for chancery
    documents for a long period, only to be replaced by the split nib scripts."

- author:
    name: ebensorkin
    picture: 109987
  body: "<cite> And if by \u2019noise\u2019 we include not only variation in letterforms
    or spacing but all those other factors that diminished the quality of so much
    printing then the manuscripts are by far the cleaner product. </cite>\r\n\r\nIf
    you get the objects themselves in front of you you will quickly see just how very
    true this is. And compared even to the vast majority of modern day printing this
    is still true. The level of detail possible is absolutely huge. \r\n\r\n<cite>
    I don\u2019t think even spacing and color forces a designer to iron out variation
    in shapes. Indeed variation in shape is often needed to achieve even color, because
    of the very different shapes of different letters. </cite>\r\nVery true. When
    I talk to people about contextual alternatives in text they often bring up the
    fear that it might produce a textuta-like over regularity of form. But I don't
    think that is remotely likely for the reasons similar to those that Bill gives
    above. A well tailored custom fit for 'Notan' will produce increased clarity re:
    the differentiation between letter shapes. Like an adjustment, an overshoot for
    example you have to have some judgement about it to achieve that effect, but like
    Bill I think equating an search for even color with literal regularity is  a false
    or at best a misleading idea. "

- author:
    name: quadibloc
    picture: 118515
  body: "@Nick Shinn:\r\n<cite>To change the example from wordspace to leading, consider
    this, set by Yale University in 1936 for Stanley Morison's text. With it's bumping
    extenders, it pays homage to many centuries of solid leading. No doubt the rules
    people will say this is a no-no, and I'm sure it would be possible to prove it's
    inefficient in a laboratory test, but hey, don't let that language go extinct.</cite>\r\n\r\nWith
    long descenders and a small x-height, leading is often superfluous. Today's typefaces
    tend to have shorter descenders and a larger x-height, offering the flexibility
    of returning to the visual effect of an older typeface by adding leading.\r\n\r\nSo,
    the fact that leading is usually used with contemporary typefaces does not mean,
    in my opinion, that not using leading with typefaces that <em>don't need it</em>
    somehow violates current typographic norms. Unless the people setting current
    typographic norms have a problem, and don't really know what they're doing."

- author:
    name: '.00'
  body: "<cite>An example of another factor in readability is darkness of the face.
    In the testing of Clearview as I remember they found that a medium weight sans
    was better than a bold for legibility in signs. I think there is also a sweet
    spot (or range) for text as far as darkness. </cite>\r\n\r\nHey Bill, if you are

    never any Medium vs Bold dilemma. The original Clearview prototypes were a 1:5.5
    stroke width to height ratio. After changing the x-height to UC relationship we
    were able to get it to a true 1:5 stroke width to height ratio. \r\n\r\nBolder
    than the original prototype. And better. This was for the 5-W and 5-W-R designs
    that were replacements for EModified."

    type is Baskerville, and it is leaded.\r\nThe pointed anachronism of the original,
    with its solid leading and bumping extenders, has fallen from favour.\r\nHistoricism

  created: '2011-09-05 04:49:40'
- author:
    name: quadibloc
    picture: 118515
  body: "@Nick Shinn:\r\n<cite>John, here is the second edition of Morison's tract,
    printed in 1967.\r\nThe type is Baskerville, and it is leaded.\r\nThe pointed
    anachronism of the original, with its solid leading and bumping extenders, has
    fallen from favour.\r\nHistoricism has evolved into a more sophisticated \"traditionalism\".</cite>\r\n\r\nDespite
    Baskerville not having short descenders like Times Roman, I'm not going to quarrel
    with the decision to use leading in this second edition. The lines are wider,
    and the proportionality between linespacing and the x-height of the typeface is
    reasonable and not excessive.\r\n\r\nThe first edition was done in a typeface
    that resembled Jenson's, perhaps Cloister Lightface. In both, the spacing between
    x-heights is roughly one x-height. The choice of typeface, the absence of leading,
    the short width of the lines, the large point size... oh, yes, \"pointed anachronism\"
    a la William Morris is certainly not entirely absent.\r\n\r\nIt's there to such
    a limited and tasteful degree, though, that my initial response is that the first
    edition is simply \"fancy\" typography, entirely appropriate to the printing of
    a short work with typography as its subject... and the second edition reflects
    a wholly pedestrian setting of body copy, equally well suited to a book of instruction
    on the use of a log-log slide rule or the maintenance of a furnace.\r\n\r\nBaskerville
    is still a beautiful typeface, and, indeed, still a bit too old-fashioned for
    many uses, but I do find fault with any typographical aesthetic that does not
    leave room for printing to adopt \"fancy dress\" or \"formal wear\" at times.
    Pedestrian typography, in contexts where conscious attention to beauty is normally
    expected, is itself jarring to the reader; while I basically agree with the \"crystal
    goblet\" viewpoint, this seems to be an example of taking it too far."
  created: '2011-09-05 05:50:54'
- author:
    name: William Berkson
    picture: 110306
  body: ">Hey Bill, if you are going to reference the Clearview stuff, at least get
    it right.\r\n\r\nAs I wrote (four years ago), I was operating from memory, and
    unfortunately mixed up some features of the story. Thanks for the partial correction.
    However, your account above also does not get the story quite right, at least
    according to [[http://clearviewhwy.com/ResearchAndDesign/legibilityStudies.php|this
    page on the Clearview Highway web site.]]\r\n\r\nAccording to the story on the
    web site, in the original tests that validated the superiority of Clearview Highway,
    the stroke width/height ratio for Clearview Highway was, as you say, 1:5.5, a
    lighter weight than the original Highway Gothic, as shown in this graphic:\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/ClearviewHighwayOrig_3771.png]
    \r\n\r\nHowever, it says your final width/height ratio was 1:5.1 (not \"a true
    1:5,\" as you write above), slightly less bold than 1:5 of Highway Gothic. Further,
    that ratio it seems is the ratio of stroke width to cap height, not to x-height.
    That is important, because the essay explains that in the final version you also
    increased the x-height as well as increasing boldness from your original 1:5.5
    ratio. Thus in the x-height region, which is critical as you use lower case, the
    ratio of stroke width to x-height is still quite noticeably less bold than Highway
    Gothic. At least this is what I conclude from the comparative graphic including
    the final version (compare top and bottom signs):\r\n\r\n[img:sites/default/files/old-images/Clearview_comparisons_5957.png]\r\n\r\nA
    final complication is that, at least as of the date of that on-line essay, the
    tests that showed the superiority of Clearview were only done on the original,
    lighter version. So we don't know how the original lighter version compares to
    the final Clearview, with somewhat heavier strokes and greater x-height. Was this
    final version ever tested against the earlier, lighter version?   \r\n\r\nSo the
    general point that Clearview Highway is less bold and more readable at a distance
    than Highway Gothic seems valid."
